Super-Resolution Methods for the BIFROST Spectrometer
Enabling Realistic Comparison Between Theory and Experiment

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
i
Abstract
This thesis develops a theoretical resolution model for the BIFROST spectrometer at the European
Spallation Source (ESS). The model, based on linear error propagation and covariance matrices, is
validated against detailed Monte Carlo ray-tracing simulations performed with McStas. It predicts the
resolution across all components of ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space with residuals below 5%, and captures the under-
lying correlations with good accuracy, though some higher-order correlations are not fully reproduced.
The model is further tested through single-mode approximation (SMA) convolutions of a lead phonon
dispersion, showing excellent agreement with McStas simulations, with full width at half maximum
(FWHM) residuals of line cuts being within 10%. In addition to its accuracy, the convolution approach
demonstrates significant computational advantages, including faster runtime and reduced memory and
storage demands. These results confirm both the validity and practical utility of the theoretical resolu-
tion model for predictive calculations and experimental interpretation.

==PAGE:7==
1 Introduction
1
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
The discovery and design of modern materials increasingly depends on understanding structure and
collective dynamics of large systems. These include atomic arrangements, phonon dispersions, spin
correlations, and electron-lattice interactions. Understanding these characteristics is essential for fun-
damental discoveries, such as unraveling the origins of high-temperature superconductivity. Along with
this, they also enable advancements in quantum materials, energy storage, and spintronic devices. As
such, accurately understanding them is important for materials research, and it is essential for both
fundamental physics and the development of next-generation technologies.
One powerful technique to explores these systems is neutron scattering. Developed in the mid 20th
century, and winning the Nobel Prize in 1994 [1], this technique stands out as not only being able to
probe bulk materials, but also able to provide a large insight into lattice vibrations and magnetic exci-
tations. However, the raw experimental data do not directly reveal the true physical spectra. Instead,
they are inherently influenced by the instrument’s resolution, effectively a filter that blurs and reshapes
the measured signal. Without rigorous modeling of this resolution, critical features such as subtle mode
splittings or fine magnetic structures risk being misinterpreted or lost altogether.
As condensed matter research delves into ever more complex and exotic materials, the demand for pre-
cise resolution characterization becomes increasingly vital. The state-of-the-art spectrometer BIFROST
at the European Spallation Source (ESS) [2], [3] will push the boundaries of what is experimentally
achievable using it’s intrinsically good resolution. Nonetheless, even with its excellent intrinsic reso-
lution, a detailed understanding of the resolution function remains crucial for correctly interpreting
experimental data and distinguishing subtle physical features.
This project will focus on developing a theoretical resolution model for BIFROST, using linear error
propagation and covariance matrices, allowing the resolution function to be represented as a multivari-
ate Gaussian. This formalism lends itself to representing the resolution as ellipsoids, which visualize the
shape and orientation. Along with this, a full resolution model can be used to perform eﬀicient convo-
lution of theoretical dispersion relations. These convolutions are essential for interpreting experimental
spectra, as they allow for quantitative comparisons that include the instrument’s resolution.
This
helps in refining theoretical models, guiding experimental designs, and understanding subtle features in
scattering data.
To complement the analytical framework, detailed Monte Carlo ray-tracing simulations are performed
with the McStas software package. These simulations provide realistic representations of the BIFROST
instrument, including its source, guide, choppers, analyzers, and detectors. The results from McStas
serve both as a benchmark for validating the theoretical resolution model and convolutions.
==PAGE:8==
2 Neutron Scattering
2
CHAPTER 2
Neutron Scattering
Neutron scattering is a powerful tool for understanding materials at the atomic scale due to a com-
bination of properties. Firstly, cold and thermal neutrons, 0.05-200 meV, have energies which lie in a
range that makes them well-suited for studying dynamics of atomic structures. Similarly, the wave-
length of the neutrons is on the order of Angstrom, the same scale as interatomic distance, allowing
for investigating atomic-scale structures. Because neutrons interact with materials quite weakly, they
have a large penetration depth, and are quite useful for studying bulk materials. Lastly, since neutrons
carry a magnetic moment, the magnetic structure and dynamics of materials is also possible to probe.
These characteristics make neutron scattering an invaluable tool for exploring a wide range of material
properties [4].
To describe neutron scatter, one would assume that quantum mechanics would be used, as they are
subatomic particles. The quantum limit for precisely knowing quantities of a given particle is given by
Heisenberg uncertainty principle:
δEδt ≥ℏ
2
δxδpx ≥ℏ
2
(2.1)
(and similarly in y and z). However, for thermal or cold neutron instruments, δxδpx will be many
times ℏ. As an example, the smallest neutron samples are typically 1 mm broad, and the divergence of
the momentum can be down to 0.06◦. Thus the uncertainty on the momentum of a 10 meV neutron is
0.001mnv ≈1.7 · 10−26 kg ∗m/s. As such the product of the two uncertainties is 1600ℏ[5]. This means
that, as a approximation, neutrons can be treated semi-classically, as particles with definite positions
and momenta during their interactions. This largely simplifies the calculations of scattering processes.
There are some important relations between different neutron characteristics that need to be defined:
E = mn
2 |v|2
(2.2)
The wavelength of a neutron from it’s de-Broglie wavelength:
λ = 2πℏ
m|v|
(2.3)
Lastly the wave number k:
⃗k = mn
ℏ⃗v
|k| = 2π
λ
(2.4)
where mn is the mass of the neutron.
2.1
Interactions and Instrumentation
Neutrons interact with materials due to two different types of interactions: Firstly, neutrons are able to
interact with atomic nuclei due to the strong force, and secondly, they are able to interact with materials
through the electromagnetic force due to their magnetic moment. For this project the interaction due
to the magnetic moment will not be considered.
There are many different situations that can take place when a neutrons and matter interact. The first
and most simple is that a nuclei absorbs the neutron. The second possibility is that no interaction
will take place, transmission. The last possibility is that the neutrons will scatter, either coherently or
incoherently. The later is used to describe randomly scattered neutrons, which do not have a directional
preference, due to interactions with the individual atoms in the material not being correlated. As such
coherent scattering is the type of scattering that is important for neutron scattering experiments, as it
arises from the interaction with individual atoms being correlated and constructively or destructively
interfering with each other.
==PAGE:9==
2.2 Elastic Coherent Scattering
3
Figure 2.1: Bragg’s law in neutron diffraction for a crystal with distance d between planes, and the
blue circles representing atoms in the crystal lattice [6].
2.2
Elastic Coherent Scattering
Most materials of interest in neutron scattering are crystalline, meaning that their atoms are arranged
in a periodic lattice. This periodicity can be described in terms of a real-space lattice with basis vectors
⃗a,⃗b,⃗c and a corresponding reciprocal basis vectors are given as:
⃗a∗= 2π
⃗b × ⃗c
⃗a ·

⃗b × ⃗c

(2.5)
where the denominator is the volume of the unit cell in real space, and similarly for ⃗b∗and ⃗c∗. For a
cubic lattice, this simplifies to be |⃗a∗| =
2π
|⃗a|2 .
When a neutron scatters off of a crystal and it’s momentum is changed, it imparts some momentum
change onto the crystal. Considering it’s incident, ⃗ki, and final wavevector, ⃗kf:
⃗ki −⃗kf = ⃗Q
(2.6)
where ⃗Q is the momentum transfer vector. For constructive interference to occur, ⃗Q must match a
reciprocal lattice vector ⃗G, leading to the Laue condition:
⃗Q = ⃗G = H⃗a∗+ K⃗b∗+ L⃗c∗,
(2.7)
where (H, K, L) are integers known as the Miller indices. These indices label a family of lattice planes.
For example, the (1, 0, 0) planes are normal to ⃗a. A plane in this family passes through all lattice
points where x = n ∗|⃗a| for a given n ∈Z, where x is the position along the ⃗a direction. Similarly, the
(1, 1, 0) plane cuts through intersects both the ⃗a and ⃗b axes.
The distance between these planes is given as
dHKL =
2π
|⃗GHKL|
(2.8)
If one considers only the scattering angle between a given plane and the plane, the Laue condition can
be written instead as Braggs Law:
nλ = 2dHKL sin(θ)
(2.9)
where n ∈N, λ is the wavelength of the neutron and θ is the scattering angle, as seen in Figure 2.1.
==PAGE:10==
2.3 Inelastic Coherent scattering
4
2.2.1
Diffractometers and Monochromators
When utilizing elastic scattering, the structure of a material is probed. By measuring the intensity
of scattered neutrons as a function of ⃗Q, it can be found which (HKL) values are allowed and thus
reconstruct the crystal structure.
Neutron diffractometers are designed for this, by recording the intensity for a set of in and out of plane
scattering angles for a given wavelength. From this, different diffraction peaks can used to map out the
structure, lattice constants and symmetry of the crystal. Along with this, it can be used to figure out
incommensurate crystal structures, which can show long-range order interactions.
An important instrumental use of elastic scattering is wavelength selection.
This can be done by
orienting a crystal at the Bragg angle, θ from (2.9). The crystal will then only reflecting whole number
multiples of the fundamental wavelength that satisfies (2.9). This is called a monochromator, and is
commonly used for energy differentiation in neutron instruments.
2.3
Inelastic Coherent scattering
When moving to the inelastic case, the neutron also imparts some energy into the sample. This energy
deposited in the sample can cause coherent excitations, which reveals the dynamics of a sample, how
the atoms and their magnetic moments change and interact over time. The momentum difference is
described the same as for the elastic method, but the energy difference is given as:
∆E = Ei −Ef
(2.10)
where Ei is the incident energy and Ef is the final energy.
2.3.1
Spectrometers
Spectrometers are instruments designed to measure ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space, thereby mapping out the dynamic
structure factor S( ⃗Q, ω) of a specific material. The structure factor encodes the probability of a neutron
exchanging both momentum ⃗Q and energy ω with the sample and provides direct information about
its dynamic excitations.
Neutron spectrometers measure different angles around the sample and fix either Ei or Ef (or both),
to measure a given ( ⃗Q, ∆E) point. For a classical triple-axis spectrometer, both Ei and Ef are fixed
using monochromators.
Figure 2.2: Top figure shows the 1D chain model one can derive the phonon energy from. The lower
figure shows the shape of the 1D phonon dispersion.
==PAGE:11==
2.3 Inelastic Coherent scattering
5
2.3.2
Phonons
An example of a collective excitation is the phonon, which is a quantized vibrational excitation. A
phonon can more simply be though of as a sound traveling through a crystal. When sound travels
through air, it compresses and expands in a synchronized, repeating fashion. Similarly, when there is a
vibration is in a crystal, the particles will coherently be displaced back and forward.
The energy of a 1D phonon can be found by considering a chain of particles of mass m, each connected
by springs with elastic constant κ, as seen in Figure 2.2 [7]. In this case, the displacement from the
equilibrium position of a particle i, can be defined as:
δxi(t) = xi(t) −xi,0
(2.11)
The equation of motion for the nth particle is then:
−2κδxn(t) + κ(δxn+1(t) + δxn−1(t)) = m∂2δxn
∂t2
(2.12)
Since the motion of the particles is expected to be periodic, it is important to impose periodic boundary
conditions, and with this, the solution will be of the form:
δxn(t) = A · exp(−iωt −ikna)
(2.13)
which leads to the dispersion relation:
ω = 2
s
κ
m sin2
ka
2

(2.14)
Where k is the wavevector of the particle, a is the unit cell size. By replacing particles with atoms,
and the spring with an attractive force between the atoms, this shows a simplified 1D acoustic phonon
dispersion relation, as shown in Figure 2.2. In real materials, this case does not work, as the unit cell
can contain more than one atom, with several interactions between several different atoms. This leads
to multiple phonon branches and anisotropies depending on the stricture of the crystal.
==PAGE:12==
3 The ESS and BIFROST
6
CHAPTER 3
The ESS and BIFROST
To fully exploit the unique properties of neutrons in probing materials, large-scale research facilities
have been developed to produce and utilize neutron beams. These facilities produce neutrons through
two different methods, either nuclear reactors, which produce neutrons as a bi-product of fission, or
spallation sources, which use large proton accelerators to collide protons with neutron rich nuclei, which
fragment into neutrons [4].
While reactor sources have enabled decades of research for many different communities, they have
reached the apex of their capabilities [8], [9]. To meet the growing demands of modern science, particu-
larly the need to study increasingly complex, dynamic, and nanoscale systems, more powerful neutron
sources are required. This need has led to the construction of the European Spallation Source (ESS) in
Lund, Sweden, designed to be the world’s most powerful neutron source.
3.1
The European Spallation Source - ESS
Currently under construction and expected to begin user operations by 2027, the ESS will utilize a
long-pulse spallation design to deliver high flux neutron beams to a suite of cutting-edge instruments.
The layout of the facility can be seen in Figure 3.1.
Neutron production at ESS starts with a powerful linear accelerator (seen in Figure 3.1, G02), using
sections of conducting and super conducting cavities to accelerate protons to 2 GeV, and an average
beam power of 5 MW. These protons strike a rotating tungsten target, which upon impact, releases
bursts of high energy neutrons. The emitted fast neutron then strike the ESS “butterfly” moderator,
which is made of two different parts: an outer cold moderator, which uses liquid hydrogen (H2) as a
moderator, and is in the shape of butterfly wings; a central X shaped moderator, connecting the cold
Figure 3.1: Layout of the ESS facility, from [10].
==PAGE:13==
3.1 The European Spallation Source - ESS
7
Figure 3.2: Simulated time structure of neutron pulses at ESS compared to existing spallation sources.
The long-pulse design provides a broader pulse width, which can be flexibly shaped by
choppers at the instrument level. From [10].
moderator wings, which uses water as a moderator. The design of the moderator produces near uniform
brightness (n/cm2/s/sr/Å) on all 44 beam lines for both thermal and cold neutrons. Thanks to this
design, ESS is expected to achieve a cold neutron brilliance three times higher per-proton than that of
other spallation sources [10].
The pulsed nature of the linac naturally leads to a pulsed neutron pulse, as seen in Figure 3.2. The linac
operates with a rate of 14 Hz, and the moderating pulse time is 2.86 ms. This combination provides a
good baseline for versatile neutron instruments, that have different requirements for the neutron beam
properties. This allows for a tailoring a ratio between the neutron flux at the sample position and
specific resolution requirements for instruments and even different experiments.
The ESS is not only designed for high performance, but also for flexibility and long-term sustainability,
supporting a broad scientific community across fields such as magnetism, soft matter, biology, and
energy materials. To meet the needs of these communities, ESS will host a comprehensive suite of
instruments dedicated to both structural and dynamical studies of materials.
The ESS’ suite includes both diffractometer such as powder diffractometer or small-angle neutron
scattering (SANS), which study atomic and magnetic structure in materials under different conditions,
Figure 3.3: BIFROST instrument layout, from [3].
==PAGE:14==
3.2 BIFROST - The Extreme Environment Spectrometer
8
Figure 3.4: TOF Diagram of the BIFROST instrument. Left shows the entire length of the instrument,
with the 3 chopper types, PSC, FOC and BW. The right figure is a close up of how the
PSC choppers shape the nominal pulse, allowing for improved energy resolution..
such as strain, temperature or magnetic field. Along with this, there is a wide array of spectrometers
for dynamics studies. One of the instruments under development at ESS is BIFROST, a state-of-the-
art spectrometer tailored for high-resolution studies of magnetic and lattice excitations in quantum
materials in extreme environments.
3.2
BIFROST - The Extreme Environment Spectrometer
As illustrated in Figure 3.3, BIFROST features a long primary flight path with flexible beam-shaping
components and a secondary spectrometer based on the prismatic analyzer concept. It is an in-direct
geometry time-of-flight spectrometer, which means that it measures the neutrons energy transfer at the
sample by having a fixed final energy, and recording the time-of-flight of each neutron when it arrives
at the detector element. By combining the high brilliance of ESS with innovative instrument design,
BIFROST is optimized for studying excitations in materials, especially superconductivity and magnetic
excitations. Specifically BIFROST has an emphasis on tailoring energy and momentum resolution for
different experiments [2].
3.2.1
Primary Spectrometer - Guide, Choppers and Jaws
The primary spectrometer transports neutrons from the moderator to the sample position, while shaping
the beam in time and divergence. It consists of a long guide system, three chopper pairs, and adjustable
collimating jaws. A simplified time-of-flight (TOF) diagram for BIFROST can be seen in Figure 3.4,
which shows the chopper system, which consists of three sets: the pulse shaping choppers (PSC), located
about 6.5 m away from the moderator; two frame overlap choppers (FOC), located at 8.5 m and 15 m
from the moderator; and the bandwidth choppers (BW), located at 78 m from the moderator.
Each of these chopper pairs serves a different purpose, which can be inferred by their names. The
PSCs shape the pulse by having a set of two choppers which have the same opening angles and speeds,
but slightly different delays. This allows one to shape the pulse in time, having a very high degree of
certainty of when the neutrons should be the center point of the two PSCs (highlighted in Figure 3.4
Right). The FOCs make sure that neutrons from different pulses do not cross into the frame of another
pulse, as seen in Figure 3.4 on the right. A frame is defined as the time interval during which neutrons
from one moderator pulse arrive at the sample position.
This interval is important, as it ensures
==PAGE:15==
3.2 BIFROST - The Extreme Environment Spectrometer
9
that the measured TOF can be associated with a specific pulse. As illustrated in Figure 3.4, a new
moderator pulse starts before the previous frame is done, which needs to be taken into consideration
when calculating the ToF. The last chopper pair, the BWCs, also serve to make sure the frames of two
pulses do not overlap, and also serve to specify the wavelength bandwidth that arrives at the sample
position, which for BIFROST is ∼1.7 Å. The choppers speeds and delays are all calculated from the
wavelength range that is requested at the sample position and the PSC opening.
Along with the choppers, the guide system helps transport neutrons from moderator to sample. The
guide consists of a focusing section before the PSC, and a curved section thereafter, to remove direct
line of sight of the moderator. This reduces the impact of fast neutrons and gamma rays that may
be created at the moderator, which would ruin measurements. After the FOCs, the guide transitions
to a de-focusing elliptical section, which gently expands the beam into a straight section before finally
refocusing it near the sample with a final elliptical part. The entire guide system is coated with varying
degrees of supermirror coating.
The coating improves neutron transport by allowing reflections at
higher incident angles than the pure substrate, utilizing alternating layers of materials to improve the
reflectivity. This also has a wavelength dependent impact on the divergence of neutrons.
Lastly, three meters before the sample position, a set of four jaws are in place which can be varied in
width. Together, the jaws act as collimators to limit the divergence of the neutrons able to arrive at
the sample.
3.2.2
Secondary Spectrometer - Analyzers and Detectors
After the sample, BIFROST employs an indirect geometry multiplexing secondary spectrometer, where
the final neutron energy, Ef, is fixed using a set of monochromators (analyzers). Five analyzer sets,
all using the (002) Bragg plane reflection of highly oriented pyrolytic graphite (HOPG) crystals have
been installed in series, selecting final energies 2.7, 3.2, 3.8, 4.4, and 5 meV. Each multiplexing analyzer
channel spans 5.2° in-plane. BIFROST’s eﬀiciency is further enhanced by employing nine such analyzer
channels in the secondary spectrometer tank, spaced 10° apart, as seen in Figure 3.5. Consequently, by
rotating the detector tank to two different angular settings, separated by 5◦, BIFROST is capable of
Figure 3.5: Left) Shows the secondary spectrometer from an aerial view. The orange circle is the
sample position. A4 shows the angle of rotation of the tank. θs shows the scattering
angle of a given neutron, into channel 1, which has central angle of ϕ0. The asymmetrical
steps in each channel is also clearly shown. Channel indices go from 1 to 9, from lowest to
highest central scattering angle. Analyzer angles go from 1 to 5, from inner most (2.7 meV)
to outer most analyzer (5 meV). Right) Shows the detector set up, with the places where
the charge is deposited to the left and right of the triplet. The top tube (Tube index
1) shows the 100 bins that each detector is split into. The arrows on the wire running
through the triplet shows the direction of Lx. As can be seen, the direction in the middle
tube (Tube index 2) is reversed compared to the others. Tubes indices go from top to
bottom, 1 to 3. Detector element indices go from left to right, 1 to 100.
==PAGE:16==
3.2 BIFROST - The Extreme Environment Spectrometer
10
Figure 3.6: A figure illustrating the multiplexing concept.
The left show the final energies of all
neutrons which land on the detector triplet, if one does not separate the tubes. The right
shows the energies if one splits the result up by tube. Here the central 4 pixels are binned
together to give this result.
covering a continuous 90° scattering angle range.
The channels follow a near-symmetric Rowland geometry, where the distance from the sample to the
analyzer is approximately equal to the distance from the analyzer to the detector. Instead of having
continuous detector coverage like CAMEA at SINQ PSI [11], [12], BIFROST employs a targeted layout
using detector triplets, each composed of three serially connected Helium-3 tubes.
Due to the mosaicity of the analyzer crystals, 60 arcmin, the accepted energies, and thus the energies
that arrive at the detector, is broadened. This has a positive effect in increasing the intensity at the
detector, but increasing the number of different energies that arrive at the detectors. However, this
allows BIFROST to utilize the prismatic analyzer concept. Since the analyzer reflects a range of energies
due to its finite mosaic spread, and these energies are reflected at slightly different angles according to
Bragg’s law, the neutrons are focused onto different angles. Thus, each detector tube in the triplet will
resolve a different final energy, as seen in Figure 3.6. This spatial-energy correlation effectively increases
the energy resolution without sacrificing intensity.
Since the analyzers are flat, only neutrons striking the center of an analyzer reflect at the exact Bragg
angle. Neutrons hitting different in-plane positions on the analyzer effectively encounter slightly different
incidence angles, which results in a variation of the probed Ef, as seen in Figure 3.7. This effect is a
function of the deviation ϕ′ from the channels central scattering angle ϕ0, and will be discussed further
in Section 3.3.
Figure 3.7: Final energy as a function of the position along the tube in degrees. 0 is at the center of
the analyzer. The red line is a function showing the cosine dependence of the distribution.
==PAGE:17==
3.3 Instrumental Calculations
11
The vertical acceptance of each analyzer is determined by the sample–analyzer distance and the mo-
saicity of the HOPG crystals. With the 7 to 9 HOPG wedges that are used in each analyzer, the total
vertical coverage of the analyzers is at most ±2° , for 2.7 meV, and lowest ±1.47° , for 5 meV.
As mentioned, the detectors are set up in triplets, which are connected in series, as seen in Figure 3.5.
Thus, signals from each tube in a triplet share a common readout system and are distinguished based
on their individual channel responses. When a neutron is detected by a 3He tube, an ionization cascade
is caused. This produces an electrical signal which propagates to both ends of the triplets. This signal
is read out as the integrated charge deposited at the left and right side of the triplet, from which both
the interaction position along the tube’s central axis can be reconstructed via the relation of the two
charges:
Lx = CR −CL
CR + CL
(3.1)
This normalized position Lx is mapped to the interval [−1,1], where −1 is the left most part of the
triplet, and 1 is the rightmost part. The physical location of the hit within the triplet, is inferred from
this value. Ignoring the resistance between the wires connecting the tubes for now, the triplet is divided
into three equal segments, with tube boundaries set at Lx = −1
3 and Lx = 1
3 for tubes 1 and 2, and 2
and 3 respectively. From this, each section can be split into 100 different bins, which are the detector
elements.
An important detail arises due to the readout wiring configuration: the tubes are connected in series
such that the right end of tubes 1 and 2 are connected, as are the left ends of tubes 2 and 3. This
means that tube 2 is electrically read out in the opposite direction compared to tubes 1 and 3, as seen
in Figure 3.5. As a result, for neutrons detected in tube 2, the position Lx must be inverted to correctly
reflect the spatial geometry of the detector.
3.3
Instrumental Calculations
To reconstruct the neutron’s energy and momentum transfer, BIFROST relies on both event-level data
and experimental metadata. Each detected neutron event provides:
• Detection time (event_time): Used to calculate time-of-flight and incident energy. referenced to
the most recent moderator pulse, meaning the zero point is defined at the start of the most recent
pulse.
• Integrated charge in the left and right ends of the Helium-3 detector triplet (event_charge_left
and event_charge_right): Allows for position reconstruction along the detector length and thus
determination of which detector element the event occurred at.
• Channel and Analyzer information (FIBER, GROUP): This information encodes which channel and
analyzer an event occurred at.
• Weighting (p): The weighting of a neutron based on detector eﬀiciency and attenuation (how
many neutrons have been absorbed by previous analyzers).
In addition to the event data, the analysis makes use of metadata recorded at the time of measurement:
• A4 Angle: Rotation of the detector tank, defined at the center of the central channel
• A3 Angle: Rotation of the sample
• PSC Velocity and Angular Phase: Determines the incident neutron time-of-flight window and
energy band
• Sample information: Describes the physical properties and environment of the sample at the
time of measurement, including temperature, magnetic field, orientation of the sample and lattice
parameters.
Together, this all allows for a complete determination of the neutron’s energy and scattering vector in
each event. The following subsections outline how the energy and momentum transfer (∆E, ⃗Q) are
calculated based on this data.
==PAGE:18==
3.3 Instrumental Calculations
12
3.3.1
Laboratory Reference Frame
Before starting any calculations it is important to define a reference frame for the instrument. As such,
for this project, the z-direction will be along the nominal incident neutron beam at the sample position,
and the y-direction will be vertically upwards. This implies that the x-direction is defined to be in the
horizontal plane, perpendicular to the beam, pointing to the left when viewed from the source toward
the sample. This reference frame is based on McStas conventions.
3.3.2
Detector Element
For the secondary spectrometer, there are a few things that are detector element specific. For BIFROST,
each tube in a triplet is split into 100 detector elements, such that in total there are
100 Detector elements × 3 Tubes × 5 Analyzers × 9 Channels = 13500
(3.2)
possible detector elements. Each of these detector elements has a nominal final energy, Ef, path length
L2, and scattering angle for a given A4. Thus it is important to know which detector element recorded
the event.
The FIBER and GROUP variables are used at the actual BIFROST instrument to know which electrical
port a signal comes from, which in turn encodes which analyzer and channel an event is coming from.
Channels and analyzer indices range from 1-9 and 1-5 respectively, as seen in Figure 3.5. They are
encoded in FIBER and GROUP as such:

integrated charges. By digitizing Lx to 300 bins, an event will have the bin id bi, and then the tube
and detector element can be calculated as:

3.4
Instrument Resolution
Resolution plays a fundamental role in determining the performance of a neutron spectrometer. For
BIFROST, achieving a good resolution for inelastic scattering is essential for precise measurements of
dispersion surfaces. Equally crucial is understanding the shape of the resolution function, how it varies in
( ⃗Q, ∆E)-space and how it varies with instrument settings and geometry. A well-characterized resolution
function is important for properly interpreting experimental data and comparing it to theoretical models.
This section will outline a theoretical framework for the resolution function R( ⃗Q, ∆E).
3.4.1
Definition of the Resolution Function
In previous sections, resolution has been loosely described as a “blurring” effect on measured data, but
it is essential to define it more precisely. In experimental physics, resolution quantifies an instrument’s
ability to distinguish between two nearby signals or features as separate entities, rather than as a single,
merged response. It reflects the intrinsic precision and limitations of the measurement system. A high
resolution instrument can resolve fine details and separate closely spaced peaks, while low resolution
leads to significant overlap and loss of information.
A good analogy for resolution is to imagine two cameras taking pictures of the night sky. One camera
has a very few large pixels. It is unable to precisely differentiate between two nearby stars, but each
pixel receives a large amount of light.
The other camera has a lot of small pixels, resolving every
star, but each pixel receives significantly less light. This illustrates the trade-off between resolution
and intensity. While a high resolution instrument will be able to resolve features better than a low
resolution instrument, the high resolution instrument will typically have a lower detected intensity, as
the number of neutrons that are within the small resolving volume in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space is fewer. This
factor is important to remember, and is the reason why if instruments had resolution functions that
were delta function in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space, they would never measure anything.
Mathematically, the intensity measured by a neutron instrument is the convolution of the intrinsic
scattering signal, represented by the dynamic structure factor S( ⃗Q, ω), and the instrument’s resolution
I( ⃗Q0, ω0) =
ZZ
S( ⃗Q, ∆E)R( ⃗Q −⃗Q0, ∆E −∆E0) d ⃗Qd∆E
(3.15)
This convolution reflects how the resolution function spreads the signal, and why both resolution and
intensity must be considered when interpreting experimental data. The resolution function of neutron
spectrometers has been extensively studied in the past, with early efforts focusing on triple-axis spec-
trometers [14], [15]. These methods involve deriving an analytical expression for the resolution at any
point in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space, by considering the resolution and correlation of incoming and outgoing wave-
vectors ki and kf. These classical methods laid the foundation for modern approaches. The resolution
calculations for direct-geometry ToF spectrometers by N. Violini et al. [16] form the basis of the present
work, which employs error propagation of ⃗Q and ∆E across the different components of the instrument.
==PAGE:21==
3.4 Instrument Resolution
15
The resolution function is well approximated by a multivariate Gaussian distribution in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space.
This assumption is justified by the central limit theorem, as the total resolution arises from a combi-
nation of multiple independent sources of uncertainty, including pulse shape, chopper timing, detector
resolution, and geometrical factors. By assuming that each of these sources contributes a roughly Gaus-
sian broadening, their convolution results in an approximately Gaussian overall distribution [17], [18].
Representing the resolution as a Gaussian allows for a compact description using a covariance matrix,
which defines both the shape and orientation of the resolution function in four-dimensional space. An-
other positive aspect of the Gaussian approach is that the resolution function can be visualized as an
ellipsoid of equal probability in lower dimensions. This ellipsoidal representation provides an intuitive
understanding of the anisotropy of the resolution, showing how uncertainties are distributed differently
along various directions in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space. This gives a direct comparison between the resolution’s ex-
tent in energy transfer versus momentum transfer and the ability to evaluate how well specific features
in the dispersion can be resolved.
3.4.2
Analytical Resolution Model
Neutron measurements probe a nominal point in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space.
However, due to instrument and
beamline uncertainties, neutrons contributing to a given measurement actually span a finite volume
around this point. To characterize the resolution, it is necessary to evaluate how small variations in
the underlying neutron parameters (e.g., path length, scattering angle, time-of-flight) and instrumental
parameters (e.g., mosaicity, geometry) propagate into variations in ⃗Q and ∆E.
This propagation is treated using linear error propagation, which assumes that the total uncertainty in
( ⃗Q, ∆E) can be approximated by the first-order derivatives of these quantities with respect to the un-
derlying parameters. This approach enables a straightforward construction of the resolution covariance
matrix, from which the Gaussian resolution function is derived. The uncertainty of a single component,
yi, in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space (either Qx, Qy, Qz or ∆E) can be written out as:
σ2
yi =
X
j
 
∂yi
∂xj

σxj

(3.16)
where xj is an independent parameter and σxj is the standard deviation from the nominal value ˆxj.
To extend this to multiple, potentially correlated, ( ⃗Q, ∆E) components, the Jacobian matrix J will be
defined with elements:
Jij = ∂yi
∂xj

Thus, J is a 4 by N matrix, where there are N parameters xj. The full covariance matrix is:
Σ ⃗Q∆E = J · Σx · J⊤
(3.18)
where Σx is the covariance matrix of the neutron path parameters. Σx is approximated to be diagonal,
and no correlations between variables are considered, which is discussed further in Section 5.3.1. The
resulting covariance matrix Σ ⃗Q, ∆E can be written as:
Σ ⃗Q, ∆E =

Here, σj is the standard deviation of component j, and ρij is the Pearson
correlation coeﬀicient between components i and j, ranging from −1 to 1, with ρii = 1 and ρij = ρji.
The diagonal terms are the variance of individual components, and the off-diagonal elements are the
covariance between different components. These covariances quantify how strongly two components of
the ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space vary together.
To compute the resolution for a specific spectrometer setting or measurement condition, it is necessary to
identify all neutron path parameters which influence the neutron’s trajectory and energy determination,
assign appropriate uncertainties to them, and calculate their partial derivatives with respect to the
output space ( ⃗Q, ∆E).
This process forms the core of the analytical resolution model, where the
sensitivity of the measurement to each parameter determines its contribution to the resolution ellipsoid.
==PAGE:22==
3.4 Instrument Resolution
16
3.4.3
Error Propagation
The analytical resolution method relies on precise knowledge of how uncertainties in the neutron path
parameters propagate to the quantities of interest. In neutron time-of-flight spectrometry, key contribu-
tors include flight path lengths, time resolution (from source pulse width or chopper opening time), and
angular resolution determined by beam divergence and detector element size. Each of these parameters
affects the measured wavevector ⃗k or energy transfer ∆E, which in turn defines the location in ( ⃗Q, ∆E)
space.
3.4.3.1
Parameter Considerations
This section will discuss which parameters will be considered for the model.
A parameter impacts
a neutron when there is possible variance in it that can cause confusion on the interpretation of the
neutrons characteristic (fx. ToF, scattering angle or final energy). In the primary spectrometer, there
are a number of physical effects which impact the neutron:
1. Path length: While nominally 162 m from moderator to sample position, or the 155.66 m from
PSC to sample, this length can be affected by the neutrons flight path. Fx. a neutron that reflects
off the guide 100 times, will have a longer flight path than one that reflects no times, as the path
will be across the guide width more. This will impact the ToF
2. PSC opening time: Depending on the PSC opening time, neutrons which do not cross the mid-
point of the PSC opening, see Figure 3.4 Left, will have a slightly different ToF. Along with this,
the time for the detector to measure the neutron will have an effect on the ToF.
3. Divergence: The nominal beam is completely parallel to the z-axis, but neutrons may have in-
plane divergence γi and out of plane divergence δi. This will affect the scattering angle.
The secondary spectrometer has 4 factors that could influence the interpretation of a neutrons charac-
teristic:
1. Path length: The nominal neutron will take the smallest path from the sample to the detector
element, but due to the mosaicity and size of the analyzers, the path length can vary, which will
vary the time of flight of the neutron.
2. Accepted scattering angle: Several different scattering angles, both in-plane, θs, and out of plane,
δf, will be accepted by the analyzers and will land on the detector element. This causes uncertainty
in the measured scattering angle.
3. Inter-planar distance: Due to the crystals not being perfect, the inter-planar distances in HOPG
are not exactly 3.355 Å. This causes uncertainty in the final energy.
4. Take-off angle: Due to physical effects of the size of the sample and detectors, and the mosaicity
of the analyzers, different energies will be able to fulfill the Bragg condition on the analyzer and
reflect onto the detector elements, which can be simplified as variation in the Bragg angle θB.
This causes there to be variations in the final energy.
For now, only the uncertainties in the individual parameters will be considered, or rather the diagonal
of Σx. Correlations, off-diagonal terms, between the parameters will be ignored for now. This will
be discussed in Section 5.3.1. In both the calculations and simulations, the sample is set to be small
(2.5 mm in width and height). Having identified the relevant parameters, the next step is to evaluate how
small variations in these values propagate through the instrument and affect the energy and momentum
transfer resolution.

of the energy transfer with respect to a parameter is given as the difference of the derivatives of the

4 BIFROST Simulations
18
CHAPTER 4
BIFROST Simulations
To validate the resolution model, this chapter introduces the McStas simulation package, its principles,
and its strengths in modeling complex neutron instruments. It details the BIFROST implementation,
event data structure, HPC workflow integration, and the applied data analysis and resolution measure-
ment methods.
4.1
McStas
McStas (Monte-Carlo Simulations of Triple Axis Spectrometers) [19], is a Monte Carlo ray-tracing
simulation software designed for modeling physical neutron transport. It provides a flexible, modular
framework for building detailed models of neutron instruments, enabling realistic simulations of neutron
experiments.
McStas works by propagating individual rays, which represent neutrons, through a series of virtual
components. Each component models a specific physical element, such as a neutron source, optics,
samples, or monitors, which govern how neutrons are transported, scattered, or absorbed. Through
its probabilistic approach, McStas captures the statistical nature of neutron interactions and produces
realistic simulations of instrument performance.
4.1.1
Monte Carlo Statistics
Monte Carlo methods are a class of algorithms which use stochastic sampling to estimate the behavior of
complex systems. Rather than attempting to solve a problem analytically, which may be impossible or
intractable due to high dimensionality or system complexity, Monte Carlo simulations rely on repeated
random sampling to obtain statistical approximations of the system’s behavior.
In essence, Monte
Carlo methods trade exactness for flexibility: They provide an approximate solution by generating large
numbers of random inputs and observing the resulting output distributions. This makes them especially
Figure 4.1: Monte Carlo estimation of π by randomly sampling points in a unit square. Left: Shows
the random points points falling inside the quarter-circle (blue), and outside (red). This
an be used to approximate π. Right: Shows the variation in the estimation of π, with
increasing sampling size N.
==PAGE:25==
4.1 McStas
19
powerful in physics, where systems often involve many degrees of freedom, and where interactions can
occur in highly nonlinear or stochastic ways.
To illustrate the principle, consider a classic Monte Carlo approach to estimating π. Suppose N points
are sampled in a unit square and the fraction of those points fall inside an inscribed quarter-circle is
calculated. The area of the square is 1, and the area of the quarter-circle is π/4. By counting the fraction
f of points that fall within the quarter-circle (i.e., those for which x2 + y2 ≤1), we can approximate
π = 4f. With enough random samples, this estimate converges toward the true value of π, despite the
randomness of the individual points. This can be seen in Figure 4.1
4.1.2
Ray Tracing
In McStas, a similar principle applies: Individual neutrons (represented as rays) are randomly generated
from a source distribution, and their paths through the virtual instrument are simulated one by one.
These rays are propagated through the simulated instrument until they reach a component, which
applies physical rules, such as scattering probabilities, absorption cross sections, or reflection conditions
to the neutron. The final set of detected events builds up a statistical representation of the instrument’s
behavior. The more rays are simulated, the better the simulation becomes.
It is important to note that the McStas rays do not represent individual neutrons, but rather rates of
neutrons. The ESS moderator will produce at peak ∼5·1013 n/cm2/s/sr/Å, as seen in Figure 3.2. This
is far too many neutrons to simulate individually, while also varying the neutron’s direction, energy and
starting position. Thus, each ray represents a neutron rate s−1.
The weight factor p, is added to the neutron descriptors. This factor is updated at each component to
represent the number of neutrons which would continue after this component in the instrument. For
example, when reflecting off a monochromator due to Bragg Scattering, some neutrons will be absorbed,
some will reflect and some will transmit through. If the probability that a neutron will be scattered is
wj, the weight factor of the neutron after the analyzer will be pj = wjpj−1, where j is the analyzers
component index. This approach makes McStas a linear simulation framework, where components are
processed sequentially, each modifying the neutron parameters in turn.
The weight multiplier wj is calculated using importance sampling. This is a method for Monte-Carlo
sampling, where instead of uniformly sampling a space, the sampling is proportional to a proposal dis-
tribution q(x). This distribution focuses the sampled points on the most “important” regions, typically
where the function being integrated has the largest contributions. An example of this would be inte-
grating the points around the mean of a Gaussian distribution more than the points outside 2σ. Each
sample in the importance sampling is assigned a weight which corrects for the biased sampling, usually
1/q(x), allowing an accurate estimate of the true distribution or integral despite the non-uniform sam-
pling. The more the proposed sampling distribution matches the integrated function, the more accurate
the importance sampling will be.
In McStas, the weight multiplier is given as fMC,bwj = Pb, where fMC,b is the importance MC sampling
probability and Pb is the physical probability of event b. This ensures that the simulation remains
physically accurate, even though it samples preferentially in more important regions of phase space [5].
4.1.3
Variance
While intensity of a neutron rate is important for experiments, equally important is the statistical
uncertainty in ones measurement. If M rays are simulated, with weights p, and probability P that
they reach a detector, the number of rays observed will be binomially distributed, such that the mean
number of observed rays will be N = MP and variance σ2(N) = MP · (1 −P). When enough rays are
simulated M ≫1, and P ≪1, which is the case for most McStas simulations, the binomial distribution
can be approximated by a Gaussian with variance σ2(N) ≈MP = N, due to the central limit theorem
[5].
When converting from observation count to intensity, I = Np, where N is number of observations, and
the variance becomes σ2(I) = σ2(N)p2 = Np2, For rays with different weight factors, pi, the intensity
and variance becomes:
I =
X
i
pi
σ2(I) =
X
i
p2
i
(4.1)
==PAGE:26==
4.2 BIFROST model
20
4.1.4
Simulations
As mentioned in the introduction, McStas simulations are made of different components, which affect
the neutrons weight factor based on physical interactions, in a linear fashion. Beyond components,
McStas also offers several powerful simulation features:
• Variables: Variables can be declared and assigned within the instrument file using DECLARE and
INITIALIZE blocks. These are useful for defining parameters, counters, or storing intermediate
results across components.
• Saving: Data from simulations can be saved using SAVE blocks, which are executed at the end of
a component’s processing. This is commonly used to record quantities like neutron flux, energy
spectra, or position distributions for analysis.
• Splitting: SPLIT allows a single neutron ray to spawn multiple copies with reduced weights,
increasing simulation statistics in regions of interest and is particularly useful for rare events.
• Conditionals: McStas also supports conditionals (WHEN) to apply to neutrons, which allows for
several branching paths to available for the neutron.
• MCPL: Integration with external particle lists via the MCPL format is also available, which allows
one to save particle positions and section off instrument parts for reuse.
4.1.5
Parallelization and HPC
The linearity of ray tracing simulations leans very much up against parallelization. Utilizing Message
Passing Interface, MPI, to split computations between multiple CPU cores, allows several simulations
to progress simultaneously. McStas supports MPI natively, enabling simulations to run eﬀiciently on
high-performance computing (HPC) clusters. This is particularly beneficial for large-scale simulations
requiring high statistical precision, where billions of neutron rays may need to be traced. The output
from all processes is automatically combined into a single set of result files.
Another beneficial aspect of utilizing HPC clusters is the ability to submit large parameter space sweeps
using small for loops to submit jobs. This approach allows users to eﬀiciently explore wide ranges of
instrument settings or sample properties by running many independent simulations in parallel. Each
job then varies a parameter systematically. Such automated batch processing significantly speeds up
design iterations and data analysis in neutron instrument development.
For this project, a general system was set up for HPC simulations, utilizing an administrator/worker
system to manage and distribute computational tasks eﬀiciently. An administrator node handles job
scheduling, monitoring, and result aggregation, while worker nodes execute the assigned simulations
in parallel. Since DTU’s HPC has a max-runtime of 72 hours, this administrator also had the role of
submitting a new administrator before it’s runtime was over, in case it did not finish it’s tasks. This
separation of roles enables scalable management of large simulation campaigns, and was crucial to
produce the large amounts of data this project utilized.
4.2
BIFROST model
To simulate BIFROST, a detailed virtual instrument was implemented in McStas, incorporating all
major components from source to detector. This model was originally built by Gregory Tucker, in a
larger project to create a digital twin of BIFROST for use when the instrument is up and running [20],
but has been adapted for this project. Below a short summary of the instrument is given.
The simulation is divided into two distinct stages, each handled by a separate McStas instrument
file. The primary spectrometer, implemented in BIFROST_1.instr, simulates neutron generation, pulse
shaping by the chopper system, and beam transport up to the sample position. The chopper system
parameters (such as delay and speed of each chopper) is calculated using Gregory Tuckers’s chopcal
library [21] to ensure the choppers allow the correct energies through, with the correct PSC open-
ing width. Neutron states are recorded in a Monte Carlo Particle List (MCPL) file at a distance of
58 cm downstream of the sample, enabling their reuse in the secondary spectrometer simulation. This
==PAGE:27==
4.3 Data Analysis
21
approach decouples the computationally expensive source simulation from the analysis of scattering
events, allowing for flexible iteration on spectrometer configurations.
The secondary spectrometer, modeled in BIFROST_2.instr, incorporates the sample, all nine analyzer
channels, and the detector array. All simulations will use a cylindrical sample with height and diameter
2.5 mm. A critical optimization involves the use of McStas’ WHEN directive to dynamically activate only
the analyzer channel intersected by a given neutron’s trajectory. This selective simulation preserves the
linearity of the simulations while maintaining the statistical correctness of the results and not simulating
any neutrons which would otherwise not be detected.
The detector dynamics is handled by Gregory Tuckers’ Detector_tubes component [22], which simu-
lates how real detector tubes produce integrated charge, and introduces a small time variance in when
the neutrons are detected (due to the finite depth of the tubes). This all helps make the simulations
as realistic as possible. Data acquisition is managed through McStas’ Monitor_nD components, which
output comprehensive event records including ToF, FIBER/GROUP variables, detector charges and
full wavevectors at the sample position (initial and final wavevector components ki,x, ki,y, ki,z, kf,x,
kf,y, kf,z). These outputs enable data analysis, as well as allowing for resolution measurements.
4.3
Data Analysis
The data analysis for this project utilized Python 3.9.21, with the matplotlib, numpy, pandas and
scipy packages. Along with this, three different classes were made to streamline the data analysis, this
section will shortly go through the classes and the implemented functionality of each. The entire code
repository can be found on GitHub [23].
4.3.1 BIFROST_nD
BIFROST_nD is the main data analysis function, utilizing the data reduction from Section 3.3. The
individual neutron event data is given and the metadata is read from the McStas files as well. Along
with this, a few pre-calculated values are read from a table at the start of the data analysis: Nominal
Ef, L2 and ϕ for each detector element. This simplifies the calculations quite a bit. From here the
following data analysis takes place:
1. self.RT_2_CA: Convert the GROUP and FIBER variables into analyzer and channel index
2. self.pixel_split: Convert the integrated charges into tube and detector element index
3. self.E_f_k_f: Load the Ef and ϕ for the given detector element. Calculate the scattering angle
from ϕ and A4. Convert Ef to |kf| and use the scattering angle to find ⃗kf. Calculate t2 from Ef.
4. self.E_i_k_i: Calculate t1 from the measured ToF, PSC opening time and t2. Find Ei and ⃗ki
from t1.
5. self.Q_hw: Calculate ∆E and ⃗Q from the above information.
6. self.a3_rotation: If A3 is in the McStas metadata, ⃗Q is rotated to the sample reference frame.
This code completes all the data analysis that is necessary for a BIFROST experiment, with the ex-
ception of converting ⃗Q to crystallographic coordinates, which should be implemented as well. All the
data is available in a pandas dataframe called from BIFROST_nD.data. All of the analysis is done for
all events at the same time, using vectorized code, making it run quite fast.
4.3.2
BIFROST_Res
BIFROST_Res is a subclass of BIFROST_nD, with a couple of extra steps:
1. self.Res_Q_hw: Read the McStas wavevectors at the sample, which will now on be refereed
to as the actual wavevector, ⃗k0

2. self.pixel_resolution: Calculate the weighted covariance of ( ⃗Q, ∆E) for all detector elements
(specific channel, analyzer, tube, detector element), where the weight of each event is p. If plot

4.4
Resolution Simulation
Simulating a resolution function using McStas requires careful consideration of how to set up the simu-
lation so that it captures the inherent instrument resolution, without introducing artificial constraints
or correlations. To do this, one must first understand what “measuring the resolution” actually entails.
In essence, resolution characterizes the spread of neutron events in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space, detected at a specific
detector element and at a specific time. That is, we are interested in the statistical variation of neutrons
arriving at a given detector element, di, at a given time ToF. This detection point corresponds to a
nominal point in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space, ( ⃗Q0, ∆E0). The resolution function describes the spread of neutron
events around this nominal point. Therefore, to estimate the resolution, we must simulate all possible
( ⃗Q, ∆E) combinations that can contribute to detections at a specific (di, ToF). The spread of these
values relative to the nominal point gives the resolution: (δ ⃗Q, δE).
To achieve this, we need to ensure that the simulated neutrons have the possibility of arriving at the
chosen detector element at the chosen time. Spatially, this means sampling all neutron trajectories
realistically reaching the detector element di. For example, each analyzer reflects neutrons over a finite
angular range. For in-plane scattering, this corresponds to a range of angles ∆θs, around the nominal
scattering angle θs. The same applies for the out-of-plane angular distribution. Thus for each incoming
⃗ki onto the sample, and random direction for ⃗kf is picked from a uniform distribution, with in-plane
angular range θs ± ∆θs and out-of-plane angular range 0 ± ∆δ.
Picking ∆θs and ∆δ can naively be done by illuminating the entire analyzer surface.
However, to
improve statistics and sample the space more eﬀiciently, there are two better strategies: Restrict the
angular range to only those parts of the analyzer which significantly contribute to the selected detector
position, and sample uniformly within this reduced angular region. Utilize importance sampling, where
angles are drawn from a Gaussian distribution resembling the analyzer’s mosaic spread. By adjusting
the neutron weight by the inverse of this Gaussian probability, the sampling becomes more eﬀicient
while still statistically valid. While the simulations described here use the first approach, the second is
a more eﬀicient way to explore the angular space and would be preferred in future simulations.
For ToF, ideally, one would only sample neutrons which arrive at the detector element at time ToF0.
However, simulating this is more diﬀicult than it initially sounds. Since the path length of each neutron
is variable, specifying a time for a neutron to land on a detector, especially after an energy transfer, is
diﬀicult. The ESS_butterfly moderator component has the ability to focus in ToF by specifying the
distance traveled and the time when the neutrons should be at this distance. Thus it should be possible
to calculate the time of arrival at a detector element, as well as what length the initial energy neutrons
would have to travel to arrive at this ToF:
T0 =
√mnL1
√2Ei
+
√mnL2
p
2Ef
Lmod = T0
r
2Ei
mn
(4.3)
==PAGE:29==
4.4 Resolution Simulation
23
Figure 4.2: Resolution function, as obtained from McStas simulations for ∆E0 = 5 meV, ⃗Q0 =
(−1.098, 0, 1.098) Å−1, Ef = 5 meV for central pixel, 0.1 ms PSC opening time.
The

corresponding variables.
However, this approach undermines the modularity of reusable primary instrument simulations and
adds complexity to the calculations. In addition, neutrons do not arrive exactly at T0, but within an
interval T0 ± δT.
By instead ToF-focusing the neutrons on to the sample position, they have a specific range of Ei ±∆Ei.
Thereafter the neutrons can be uniformly spread in the energy range Ef ±∆Ef. As such the modularity
is preserved. If one focuses the time of arrival at the sample position to be within ∆T = 1µs, this will
cause there to be a certain time range within which the neutrons will arrive at the detector element. For
5 meV analyzer, this time is ∼37 µs. This is much smaller than the ToF uncertainty due to the PSC
opening time, and is of a similar order of magnitude to the detection time uncertainty. Thus, integrating
over this entire time width is a fine first approximation for verifying the theoretical resolution model.
In the future, the previous method of time focusing on the detector would be preferred, but could be
more computationally expensive.
By sampling broadly across these angle and energy distributions (θs, δ, Ef), McStas can simulate the
full spread of ( ⃗Q, ∆E) values contributing to a given detection event, from which the resolution function
can be constructed. Figure 4.2 shows an example of the resulting resolution distribution.

5 Resolution Results and Validation
24
CHAPTER 5
Resolution Results and
Validation
To assess the validity of the resolution model developed in Section 3.4.2, it is essential to compare its
predictions with detailed Monte Carlo simulations of the BIFROST instrument. This chapter presents
the determination of neutron path variances used in the theoretical model, Σx, explores how different
instrumental parameters influence the resolution, and evaluates the model’s accuracy by comparing it
to McStas simulation results. The comparison provides insight into the reliability of the model and
identifies the dominant contributions to the overall resolution.
Note: Due to storage issues on the HPC system shortly before the project deadline, resolution data
for the 2.7 meV analyzers was lost for PSC opening times other than 0.2 ms.
5.1
Determination of Neutron Path Variances
To determine the variances of the neutron path parameters, as discussed in Section 3.4.2, it is easiest
to treat them independently, and simulate the variance in each of them. The nine parameters which
will be studied at are the ones from the theoretical resolution model in Section 3.4.3.1. The variances
of any secondary spectrometer parameters will be done for the central detector element of the central
tube and channel of a given analyzer. All variances are obtained by calculating the second moment, or
variance, of the simulated distributions:
σ2

(5.1)
where x is the parameter, N is the number of samples, xi is a given sample, and µ is the mean, or
first moment. This is a weighted variance calculation, where each neutron event contributes it’s weight
factor from MCStas. In cases where the distributions appear Gaussian, this has been validated by
fitting Gaussian functions to confirm consistency.
Figure 5.1: Left) Path Lengths in the Primary Spectrometer instrument, from the mid point of the

5.1 Determination of Neutron Path Variances
25
5.1.1
Primary Spectrometer Flight Path Length - L1
Variations in the primary spectrometer flight path length L1 arise primarily from neutron beam di-
vergence and the number of reflections off the neutron guide walls, which increase the effective travel
distance. This effect can be simulated by recording the time at which a neutron passes the midpoint
between the two PSCs and the time it reaches the end of the primary spectrometer component in the
McStas model, then multiplying the time with the velocity of the individual neutron to get the length.
This result is illustrated in Figure 5.1 Left. The resulting distribution of L1 shows some spread and
approximately follows an exponentially decaying form.
This behavior contradicts a the assumption in the theoretical resolution model that all parameters can
be approximated as Gaussians. This will be discussed further in Figure 5.20, but since the uncertainty
in L1 is so small, it won’t have much of an effect for now. For L1, the standard deviation, or square
root of the variance, used in Σx is σL1 =4 mm, which is a very small variance on a distance of 155.66 m.
There is a slight energy dependence of L1 and it’s variance, but this variation is so small that it will be
ignored for now.
It is also worth noting the nominal distance from the PSC to the sample is 155.66 m [2], which differs
by about 8 cm from the average simulated value.
This problem was experienced during the entire
simulation, but is likely a small mistake in the simulations.
5.1.2
Secondary Spectrometer Flight Path Length - L2
Simulating the flight path length in the secondary spectrometer is more complex than for L1. It is
done by replacing the sample component in the secondary spectrometer instrument file with a highly
divergent, point-like source (a circular source with a radius of 2.5 mm), which illuminates the entire
analyzer. By measuring the time from this artificial source to each detector element, the corresponding
flight paths can be inferred in the same manner as for L1.
The resulting distribution is shown in
Figure 5.1 Right.
This distribution is not Gaussian either, rather a flat topped or super-Gaussian distribution. Never-
theless, the standard deviation used σL2 ≈5 mm was used in the resolution covariance matrix. This
corresponds to about 0.2% of the nominal L2 value.
5.1.3
Interplanar Distances - d
The variations in interplanar distances in the analyzers is not something that can be directly simulated,
but will still be approximated from knowing the quality of the HOPG analyzer slabs. For this, a rough
estimate of 0.1% variation of the interplanar distance of 3.355 Å was used, simply due to the fact that
this variation is so small, it should not play a part in the simulation. This effect is expected to be
negligible in practice, but it is included for completeness in the covariance matrix.
5.1.4
Time of Flight and Take of Angle - T & θB
Variance in the time of flight can be attributed to 3 factors: When the neutron travels through the
midpoint of the PSC opening; when during the flightpath through the detector the neutron is detected;
and when during the flightpath through sample the neutron scattered. If one ignores the sample effects
on resolution, the prior two can be split up into two independent effects of the ToF variance.
The timing variance introduced by the PSC is governed by the chopper opening width, which in turn
depends on the rotation speed and relative delay of the PSC. As the chopper spins faster, the opening
time becomes narrower, reducing the timing variance. However, this relationship is nonlinear and also
depends on the delay, and the time distribution deviates significantly from a Gaussian shape. Along
with this, when the chopper spins more slowly, the opening becomes wider, leading to increased variance
and a more trapezoidal distribution.
The time at which a neutron is detected depends on the point at which the neutron interaction within
the detector tube. Since neutron capture can occur at any point along the neutron’s trajectory through
the gas volume, this introduces an inherent timing variance. For a fixed neutron energy, this effect is
==PAGE:32==
5.1 Determination of Neutron Path Variances
26
Figure 5.2: Energy Resolution σ∆E when changing ∆E, for different PSC opening times. The top
plots show the resolution curves, as well as the contribute (Cont.) of different neutron
path parameters. The error-bars on the simulation values comes from it being the average
over many simulations at different angles. The lower curve shows the residuals of the
curves R = (σsim −σtheory)/σsim for different PSC opening times.
approximately constant and independent of other parameters, and is ∼10 µs. Compared to even the
smallest PSC opening time, this variance is minimal, and can be ignored.
In the secondary spectrometer, the variance in the neutron’s take-off angle, θs after scattering from
the analyzer arises from two effects: geometric spread (finite sample, analyzer and detector sizes) and
energy dispersion due to analyzer mosaicity. Because different energies can satisfy the Bragg condition
at slightly different angles, there is a spread in the reflection angle for a given detector position. While
this is diﬀicult to compute analytically, it is constant for each detector element within a fixed analyzer
set.
Since all other contributions to the energy resolution have been estimated, the remaining timing variance
attributed to the PSC can be extracted directly by comparing the simulated energy resolution to the
theoretical prediction. By fitting both σθB and σT across different energy transfer for different PSC
opening times (dt), one expects σθB to remain constant across all datasets, while σT oF is constant for
each opening time.
As seen in Figure 5.2, this approach yields excellent agreement across a wide range of PSC opening
times and energy transfers, with only minor residuals between simulation and theory. The figure also
breaks down the contributions from the individual parameters to the overall resolution. The simulation
points in Figure 5.2 represent averages over 24 simulations at different in-plane scattering angles θs,
which were primarily used for evaluating ⃗Q resolution effects. The observed large increase in residuals
for a PSC opening time of 0.1 ms may be attributed to statistical noise, as the reduced opening time
allows fewer neutrons to pass through the choppers and reach the relevant detector element. While
these effects are important, there may also be a dependence on “when” in the wavelength band the
energy ∆E0 is, but this should be considered in a further analysis of the resolution function.

5.1.5
Incident Divergence - γi & δi
The angular spread of neutrons arriving at the sample, characterized by the incident horizontal diver-
gence γi and vertical divergence δi, is a key contributor to the ⃗Q resolution. These divergences are
primarily defined by the geometry and collimation of the incoming beam, as well as the moderator and
guide characteristics upstream of the sample.
Quantifying the incident divergence is, in principle, straightforward: One can place a McStas moni-
tor 1 mm at the sample position, and histogram the horizontal and vertical divergences of the beam.
However, a number of subtleties must be considered.
The critical angle for total internal reflection from neutron guides is energy dependent, introducing
a cutoff on the divergence spectrum. Lower-energy neutrons can be reflected at larger angles than
higher-energy neutrons, resulting in a coupling between energy and angular distribution. This means
the maximum attainable divergence decreases with increasing neutron energy. This energy dependence
is clearly seen in Figure 5.3, where the divergence in both horizontal and vertical directions is plotted
as a function of incident energy.
The horizontal divergence shows an inverse trend, which is quite easy to characterize. However, the fit
of σγi is not perfect for energies between 10 and 45 meV, which might hint towards there being more
complex effects that are at play here, which would need to be more closely looked at. The vertical
divergence appears to plateau at approximately 0.6◦up to 10 meV, after which it decreases.
This
Figure 5.3: Top) Shows how the horizontal and vertical divergence standard deviation changes with en-
ergy, for the x ∈[−2.5, 2.5]mm on the sample position. Bottom) Shows the distribution of
horizontal divergence γi and vertical divergence δi for Ei =10 meV, for x ∈[−2.5, 2.5]mm.
==PAGE:34==
5.1 Determination of Neutron Path Variances
28
Figure 5.4: Heatmap of divergence in degrees vs. x-position for different jaw positions. Left) Jaws
completely open; Middle) Jaws set such that FWHM of divergence at x = 0 is ±0.4◦;
Right) Jaws set such that FWHM of divergence at x = 0 is ±0.2◦. The colorbars are
shown without ticks, as the intensity scale is arbitrary; each plot represents a small sample
of neutron rays, 10 million, that would reach the sample position under the given jaw

behavior may be due to geometric constraints in the vertical plane of the guide system, which limits
the divergence for low energies. As such, the characterization of σδi was split up, where the variance
on divergences for energies lower than 10 meV was set to 0.6◦, while for higher energies it was set to be
inversely proportional to increasing energies, like the horizontal divergence. From Figure 5.3 Bottom
Left, it is also clear that the vertical divergence does not follow a Gaussian distribution either. Further
work on characterizing this parameter would be important for the resolution model.
The jaws, acting as adjustable collimators upstream of the sample, play a significant role in defining
both the horizontal spatial and angular profile of the beam. Slimmer jaws restrict the beam divergence,
leading to a more collimated beam at the sample position but also reduces the intensity. Moreover, the
divergence is not spatially uniform across the sample surface; positions near the edges may experience
different divergence characteristics due to how the guide focuses in on the sample position. Figure
Figure 5.19 illustrates how varying jaw openings influences the divergence distribution as a function of
position, highlighting the trade-off between beam intensity and angular resolution.
Importantly, the jaws impose a hard upper limit on the divergence of neutrons reaching the sample. This
means that neutrons with natural divergence exceeding ±0.4◦or ±0.2◦will be physically blocked when
the jaws are set to those respective FWHM limits. As a result, the divergence distribution becomes
truncated, particularly affecting lower-energy neutrons whose divergence would otherwise exceed the
allowed range.
5.1.6
Accepted Scattering Angle - θs & δf
The scattering angle of a neutron detected after interaction with the sample is another key factor in
determining the resolution of a neutron spectrometer. These angles, θs, in-plane, and δf, out-of-plane,
represent the final trajectory of the neutron relative to the nominal beam axis, z, and are shaped by
both the sample-to-detector geometry and the analyzer-to-detector geometry.
As was shown in the BIFROST article [2], an approximation of the in-plane resolution can be found,
by adding the in-plane angular contributions in quadrature:
∆θs ≈
p
s2 + (2ξLad sin(θB))2 + d2
Lsa + Lad
(5.2)
where s is the FWHM sample size, ξ is the mosaicity, d is the detector resolution and Lsa and Lad are the
nominal distances from the sample to analyzer, and analyzer to detector, respectively. This expression
==PAGE:35==
5.1 Determination of Neutron Path Variances
29
Figure 5.5: Left) Histogram of which scattering angles neutrons have at the sample position, which are

article for a 5 mm sample, while the simulations were for a 2.5 mm sample. The black dots
in the back show the values for each of the 27 simulations sampled for this plot.
provides a useful first-order estimate of the angular resolution in the scattering plane, capturing the
key geometric and optical contributions of the secondary spectrometer. McStas simulations confirm the
validity of this, with a small deviation from the results of the BIFROST article, see Figure 5.5. This
small change is due to the fact that the BIFROST article calculates the angular deviation with a 5 mm
tall sample, while these simulations are with a 2.5 mm tall sample.
The accepted out-of-plane scattering angle initially seems more complex to characterize. Each analyzer
blade contributes differently to the vertical angular acceptance depending on its position, number
of slabs, and tilt.
Instead, McStas simulations were used to evaluate the vertical scattering angle
distribution δf, providing a more accurate characterization of the out-of-plane resolution, as seen in
Figure 5.6. In the simulations, similarly to how the variance on L2 was found, the entire analyzer was
illuminated by a source at the sample position, and then the initial angles of which neutrons hit the
detector element was calculated. The resulting variance shows a similar dependence on final energy as
in Figure 5.5, suggesting that an expression similar to (5.2) might also describe the out-of-plane case
well.
