5.2 Validation of Theoretical Model
30
5.2
Validation of Theoretical Model
Having quantified the variances on the neutron path parameters, the next step is to validate the theo-
retical resolution model against McStas simulations across a range of instrumental configurations. To
ensure comprehensive validation, a representative parameter space spanning key experimental degrees
of freedom is set:
• Scattering Angle: The model must be valid across the full angular range accessible on BIFROST,
[5◦, 135◦] with 5◦steps. For high PSC opening time or high energy transfer, only [5◦, 45◦, 95◦, 135◦]
was used.
• Energy Transfer: The resolution model is tested across a broad range, [0, 50] meV. To maximize
data coverage, all incident energies corresponding to integer energy transfers for a single analyzer
are used for all analyzers.
This approach effectively quintuples the measurement points - for
instance, using the ∆E =10 meV setting from the 2.7 meV analyzer, allows the 5 meV to measure
at ∆E =7.7 meV.
• PSC Opening Time: As discussed in Section 5.1.4, the Pulse Shaping Chopper significantly im-
pacts energy resolution. The model is validated for different opening durations: [0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 1.5]
ms.
• Jaws: As shown in Figure 5.19, the jaws shape the divergence profile and therefore the resolution.
This is tested for open, ±0.4◦and ±0.2◦jaws.
• Detector Element: Path variances were previously calculated only for central detector pixels.
The performance of the model for off-center detector elements, across tubes and analyzer channels,
is also tested.
5.2.1
Projections and Cross Sections
To extract the resolution of an individual parameter from the full resolution model, one must extract
the covariance matrix Σ ⃗Q,∆E. The covariance between two components of ( ⃗Q, ∆E), is calculated using
(4.2) with i ̸= j.
The following sections will focus on 1D resolution components, which is obtained by projecting the full
4D resolution function onto individual axes. This approach is justified by a key property of multivariate
Gaussian distributions: although the full distribution may include correlations between variables (cap-
tured by off-diagonal terms in the covariance matrix), the marginal distribution of any single variable
depends only on its variance. A marginal distribution describes the behavior of one variable regardless
of the others. As a result, projecting the resolution function onto a single axis yields a well-defined and
interpretable measure of resolution along that direction.
In a similar way, 2D projections involve selecting a 2×2 submatrix of the full covariance matrix. These
submatrices describe the shape and orientation of resolution ellipses in chosen planes, such as (Qx, Qz)
or (∆E, Qy), and are used to visualize correlations and anisotropies in the resolution function. An
example of such 2D projections is shown in Figure 4.2, where the structure of the resolution function
in the different planes can be directly interpreted.
Cross sections, the conditional distribution over a subset of components given fixed values of the others,
are just as important for resolution. Cross sections allow for the examination of the effective resolution in
the vicinity of a feature, taking into account how the uncertainty is reduced by fixing some components.
For a multivariate Gaussian distribution, the conditional (cross-sectional) covariance is computed using
the Schur complement[24]. Given a covariance matrix:
Σ =
Σaa
Σab
Σ⊤
ab
Σbb

(5.3)
where Σaa is size N × N and is the covariance for variables a, Σbb is size M × M and is the covariance
for variables b, and Σab is size N ×M and is the covariance between a and b. The conditional covariance
of a given b is:
Σa|b = Σaa −ΣabΣ−1
bb Σab.
(5.4)
==PAGE:37==
5.2 Validation of Theoretical Model
31
This expression reflects the reduction in uncertainty due to conditioning: when some variables are fixed
(those in b), the variance in the remaining ones (those in a) generally becomes smaller, because part of
their variability was correlated with b.
All together, this forms the foundation for evaluating and validating resolution performance across the
various components of ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space. With these tools in place, the specific results for each resolution
component is shown.
5.2.2
∆E Resolution
The 5 meV analyzer energy resolution results are presented in Figure 5.2. At low PSC opening times
and for low energy transfers (<10 meV), the resolution is dominated by the secondary spectrometer.
In contrast, as either the energy transfer or PSC opening time increases, the primary spectrometer
becomes the dominant contributor to the overall energy resolution.
Additionally, the effects of path length variance (L1 and L2) and interplanar spacing (d) variation were
found to be negligible across the studied parameter space. This supports their omission in simplified or
computationally eﬀicient versions of the theoretical resolution model.
The simulated data comes from averaging over several simulations that are done at different scattering
angles θs.
The error bars, from the standard deviation, on the simulation data are notably small
suggesting the energy resolution is largely independent of the scattering angle as one would expect.
The strong agreement between the theoretical prediction and simulation across the full energy transfer
range demonstrates the robustness of the model, even as the relative contributions of the primary and
secondary spectrometers shift. This holds true for the other analyzers. The residuals consistently show
a slight negative bias, where the model over estimates the resolution by ∼1-2%. This is a tradeoff that
will be talked about later, in relation to the in-plane ⃗Q resolution. The residuals are used to compare
the model to the simulated data, where it is defined as:
R = (σsim −σtheory)/σsim
(5.5)
Figure 5.7: Out-of-plane Momentum Transfer Resolution σQy, when changing ∆E for different PSC
opening times. The top plots show the resolution curves, as well as the different com-
ponents that contribute to it. The error-bars on the simulation values come from the
standard deviation of the average over many simulations at different angles. The lower
curve shows the residuals of the curves for different PSC opening times.

5.2.3
Qy Resolution
The 4.4 meV analyzer Qy resolution results are shown in Figure 5.7. As with the energy resolution
results, the Qy resolution is found to be largely independent of both scattering angle and PSC opening
time. Instead, the dominant factor influencing the resolution is the incident energy, which, as discussed
in Section 5.1.5, significantly affects the incident beam divergence.
A distinct “knee” in the resolution curve is observed here as well, though it appears at slightly higher
energy values than would be expected based on the divergence trends in Figure 5.3. Applying a corrected
model for the incident divergence, similar to the correction used for the energy resolution and time-of-
flight variance, yields a much closer match between the theoretical prediction and the simulated results.
The remaining discrepancy may stem from correlations not fully captured in the model. For example,
the nontrivial shape of the incident angle distribution δi (Figure 5.3, bottom right), which becomes
convolved with the similarly non-uniform δf distribution (Figure 5.6). Nonetheless, the model captures
the overall behavior of the out-of-plane resolution very well.

5.2.4
In-plane ⃗Q Resolution
The in-plane resolution is split into two parts, Qx and Qz, which can be seen in Figures 5.8 and 5.9.
The resolution in Qz is by far the most complex among all ( ⃗Q, ∆E) components. As seen in Figure 5.8,
the ToF variance plays a significant role at low (< 15◦) scattering angles, as well as at high scattering
angles.
Additionally, near θs = 90◦, the variance in the scattering angle becomes dominant.
This
is expected, since the resolution is proportional to ∂Qz
∂θs ∝−sin(θs). At low scattering angles, small
energy transfers, and narrow pulse-shaping chopper (PSC) openings, the take-off angle variance σθB
becomes the primary limiting factor. The overall resolution model performs remarkably well in this case.
The slight underestimation of the energy resolution discussed in Section 5.2.2 is balanced by improved
agreement in most of the Qz resolutions. There may be a minor overestimation of the time-of-flight
(ToF) variance, which could contribute to residual discrepancies. However, given the large number of
varying parameters in the model, achieving a perfect match remains challenging.
While Qz exhibits the most individual contributions, the Qx resolution is more diﬀicult to characterize,
==PAGE:40==
5.2 Validation of Theoretical Model
34
primarily due to the influence of incident beam divergence. The characterization of σγi does result in a
good match between the theoretical resolution model and the simulations, as seen in Figure 5.9 Bottom.
There seems to be a power-relation (linear in log-space) between ℏω and the Qx resolution, which was
not the characterization of σγi. This could hint that the characterization of σγi needs to be corrected
for the model. Outside of the γi contribution, the θs contribution sinks from θs = 5◦to θs = 95◦, since
∂Qx
∂θs ∝cos(θs), while the θB rises since ∂Qx
∂θB ∝sin(θs). These trends describe the general fall and rise of
the Qx resolution. This shows that there simply needs to be a better description for the γi contribution
to the resolution.
The model exhibits consistent strengths and weaknesses across all analyzers.
5.2.5
Covariance Matrix
Beyond 1D resolution components, the full 4D resolution matrix characterizes correlations between
∆E and ⃗Q.
This section compares the covariance matrices derived from simulation and from the
analytical model to assess how well joint uncertainties are captured. While direct inspection of these
matrices can highlight discrepancies in shape and orientation, it remains challenging to reduce such
a high-dimensional comparison to a single, interpretable quantity. To gain insight into how well the
off-diagonal structure (i.e., correlations) is captured, the Pearson correlation coeﬀicient of the flattened
upper triangular elements for the theoretical and simulated covariance matrices is calculated:
ρij = σij
σiσj
(5.6)

5.2.5.1 Qy Correlations
In the theoretical model, the only two neutron path parameters which impacted the Qy resolution were
the out-of-plane incoming and outgoing angles, δi and δf. These two parameters uniquely contribute
to Qy, and Qy has no other contributions. As such the theoretical model predicts that there is no cor-
relation between any other component and Qy. However, the simulation does reveal slight correlations
between Qy and the remaining components, as shown in Figure 5.10.
Unlike the 1D resolution components, the error bars on the average correlations are considerably larger,
making it more diﬀicult to define a general trend. Nonetheless, som general trends can be found. Most
notably, there is a clear positive trend in ρQy∆E, starting at values below 0, and tends towards 0 with
higher energy transfers. This could be due to the fact that higher incident energies have lower vertical
divergence, and therefore there will be a lower correlation between these two values at higher energy
transfers.
There is a very slight linear trend for ρQxQy, although the large error bars limit confidence
in this conclusion. Lastly, ρQyQz does not seem to have any specific trend, rather being very close to,
or slightly lower than, 0 for most energy averaged values. Across all PSC opening times, there does not
seem to be any change, only an improvement in statistical precision, as reflected in the reduced error
bars.
Figure 5.11 shows the trend of the correlations with respect to the scattering angle. While there is no
discernible trend for ρQxQy or ρQy∆E, there is a slight positive trend for ρQyQz. Therefore it is not
impossible to suggest that there might be some correlation between the horizontal beam divergence and
vertical beam divergence. The correlation might have a proportionality to a −cos curve, as it is negative
for small angles, and positive for angles > 90◦, which might suggest that it has a correlation with the
take off angle derivative, which is proportional to the same value. Why the correlation ρQx Qy does not
have a similar correlation is odd, but it might suggest that the correlation is caused by something else,
or that Qx is much smaller than Qy.
How important these correlations are is a bit uncertain, as BIFROST integrates the entire Qy component
in it’s measurements anyways. Along with this, these correlations are very small, and correspond to a
very small tilt in the relevant axes. Nonetheless it is important to have in mind when modeling any
BIFROST experiments.
5.2.5.2 Qx Correlations
The correlations between Qx and the two other components, Qz and ∆E, have 3 and 2 neutron path
parameters in common, respectively. As such, the theoretical model does produce a predicted correlation
between the components, which varies greatly for different angles, PSC Opening time and energy
transfer.
As can be seen in Figure 5.12 Top, the model is consistent at predicting ρQxQz for low energy transfers,
but seems to fail as it gets higher. This is more clear for lower PSC opening times, as it slightly under
estimates the correlation. This could be an effect of the high residuals on σQx at low energy transfers.

However, this does not explain the large discrepancies between simulations and theory for lower PSC
opening times. This may be some missing correlation that has not been considered for the model. As
can be seen for 0.2 ms opening time, there is a large discrepancy at high energy transfers, where the
correlation seems to be more positive than expected.

5.2.5.3 Qz-∆E Correlations
The last correlation to look at is the correlation between Qz and ∆E. Despite these two components
sharing a strong dependence on common neutron path parameters, the theoretical model reproduces
the observed correlations remarkably well, as shown in Figures 5.14 and 5.15. Interesting aspects of
the figures to note is that the correlation for small scattering angles or high energy transfers is close
to 1. This indicates strong correlations under those conditions. Second, increasing the PSC opening
time consistently drives the correlation closer to 1 at lower energy transfers. This is expected, as longer
opening times make the time-of-flight (ToF) variance the dominant contributor to both the energy and
Qz resolutions, thereby tightly linking them. This fits with the angular results, where higher PSC
openings causes there to be a lower angular dependence.
Figure 5.15: Correlations between Qz and ∆E for scattering angles, for the 3.8 meV analyzer and
varying PSC opening. The crosses × represent the simulated data at these angles and
the dashed lines represent the theoretical models predictions, where color indicates energy
transfer.
==PAGE:44==
5.2 Validation of Theoretical Model
38
There is also an interesting aspect in the fact, that there is a “phase transition” when the PSC opening
time is between 0.5 ms and 1 ms, where the correlation no longer becomes negative. This transition
suggests a shift in the balance of dominant resolution contributions across this range of PSC timings.
5.2.6
Visualization of Resolution Ellipsoids
As discussed in Section 5.2.1, it is possible to create two-dimensional (2D) projections of the resolution
function in selected variable planes. This approach provides an intuitive way to visualize and compare
the theoretical and simulated resolution.
The method involves projecting both covariance matrices
onto the chosen 2D subspace and representing the 2σ contour as an ellipse derived from each covariance
matrix. These contours effectively illustrate the shape and orientation of the resolution ellipsoid in the
selected plane, highlighting correlations between the chosen variables. By plotting the contours for both
the simulated and theoretical cases on the same graph, one obtains a direct visual comparison of their
agreement. Along with this, the 2D cross section, calculated using the Schur complement, will also be
shown to compare the covariance matrices.
While such visualizations are highly useful for gaining qualitative insight, they can sometimes be mis-
leading. Therefore, a complementary metric is essential to objectively evaluate the degree of agreement
between the theoretical and simulated covariance matrices.
5.2.6.1
Bhattacharyya Distance
The Bhattacharyya distance provides a scalar metric to quantify similarity between two multivariate
distributions [25]. Here, it is used to compare the simulated and theoretical resolution functions, ac-
counting for both spread and correlation.
For two n-dimensional Gaussian distributions N0(⃗µ0, Σ0) and N1(⃗µ1, Σ1), the Bhattacharyya distance
is defined as:
DB = 1
8(⃗µ1 −⃗µ0)T Σ−1(⃗µ1 −⃗µ0) + 1
2 ln

det Σ
√det Σ0 · det Σ1

(5.7)
where Σ = 1
2(Σ0+Σ1). This metric offers a compact summary of how well the theoretical and simulated
resolution functions agree. A smaller DB indicates closer agreement, DB = 0 indicates an identical
distribution, while larger values signify differences in either shape (covariance) or alignment (mean
offset). In this context, the Bhattacharyya distance is used as a quantitative benchmark for model
validation across different instrument settings, utilizing only the later term, as the covariance is the
values of interest, and the prior term will be 0, as µ is the same for the two distributions, ( ⃗Q0, ∆E0).
The Bhattacharyya distance is not without limitations. It conflates discrepancies in spread and orienta-
tion into a single value, which can obscure the nature of the mismatch. This is particularly important
when the covariance matrices differ only slightly in shape but not in determinant. Along with this,
there is no predefined “good” value for the distance, rather it depends on the application. In Machine
Learning applications, a distance of < 0.5 is good enough for classification. However, for this project,
the simulated and theoretical covariance matrix should be as close as possible to each other, so a limit
for a good distance is set to be < 0.01.
5.2.7
Resolution Ellipses
A full overview of all DB values can be found in Appendix C, but notable trends and results will be
presented here, alongside the resolution ellipse plotted for these values.
Out of a total of 6,369 total points looked at only 122, or 1.9%, of these values were above the set DB
boundary, as seen in Figure 5.16. These outliers fall into two distinct categories: (1) Anomalous points
likely due to simulation artifacts (2) Points corresponding to very high energy transfers.
The first category consists of two points with the highest DB values (0.85 and 0.30). Both exhibit
McStas integrated intensities approximately 50 times higher than neighboring points differing by only
±5◦in scattering angle, an implausible result suggesting numerical or simulation errors. These points
are therefore excluded from further interpretation.
==PAGE:45==
5.2 Validation of Theoretical Model
39
Figure 5.16: Left: Histogram of DB values for all points looked at. The green line at 10−2 shows the
DB boundary set, while the red line shows the boundary used for Machine Learning.
The second category comprises points with ∆E > 20 meV, particularly at low scattering angles (e.g.,
5◦). The highest remaining DB value is illustrated in Figure 5.17. Here the high DB value can be
attributed to the slight tilt difference of the ellipsoids in the Qz-∆E projection. This corresponds to
a small residual in the energy variance: Simulations yield σ2
∆E = 1.22 meV2 compared to the model’s
σ2
∆E = 1.16 meV2—a difference of about 5%. While the resulting angular difference between the two
distributions, 0.1◦, may seem negligible, since the distribution in Qz-∆E is quite elongated and narrow,
this difference is amplified, further impacting the distributions’ overlap.
The reasoning for the off tilt, and more generally, the larger DB values for higher energy transfer,
values at higher energy transfers could be explained by statistical sampling. Since the same number of
neutron events is simulated for both low and high energy transfers, the broader resolution functions at
high ∆E are relatively under sampled. Consequently, a large Gaussian requires more events to achieve
the same statistical fidelity as a smaller Gaussian. With insuﬀicient sampling, the covariance estimation
becomes noisy, which can manifest as slight covariance deviations. This hypothesis is explored further
in Section 5.3.2.1.
While the edge cases are important, the general result of the model shows large agreement with simula-
tions. In most configuration, like the one seen in Figure 5.18, the model captures not only the size, but
also the shape of the resolution function. Minor residuals in Qx resolution, as discussed in Section 5.2.4,
account for most of the remaining differences. Overall, these results confirm that the theoretical reso-
lution model provides an accurate and reliable description of the instrument response across a broad
Figure 5.17: Resolution Ellipses, 2σ contour of the Gaussian, for the non-anomalous point with the
highest DB value. Left to right: Projection onto Qx and Qz; Projection onto Qx and ∆E;
Projection onto Qz and ∆E. ∆represents the difference from the nominal measurement
point.

range of operating conditions.
5.2.8
Varying Detector Elements and Jaws
Finally, the robustness of the resolution model is tested, by applying it to different detector elements,
including those far from the central channel axis, as well as different jaw openings. This ensures the
model’s applicability across the full detector bank and validates spatial inhomogeneities in resolution.
5.2.8.1
Detector Element Varying
The results of this analysis can be seen in Appendix C.1, where the Bhattacharyya distance is tabu-
lated for different channels central detector element and different tubes for different detector elements
respectively. All the calculations and simulations were done for the relevant detector element being
placed at 50◦, with scattering angle 50◦, and a PSC opening time of 0.2 ms.
The agreement between the model and simulation is generally very good. Most discrepancies arise from
a slight tilt misalignment in the Qx–∆E projection, and, at low energy transfers, an underestimation
of the Qx resolution. These deviations are consistent with the earlier findings on resolution widths and
correlation structure. Importantly, all Bhattacharyya distances remain below 2 ×10−3, with agreement
improving at higher energy transfers. No significant systematic variation is observed when switching
away from the central pixel. This holds true across different tubes or detector elements, despite potential
differences in their take-off angle variances. This suggests that the resolution model remains robust
across the detector, which simplifies calculations significantly.
5.2.8.2
Jaws
The last instrument parameter that is changed for the validation part of this project, is the jaws. So far,
the jaws have consistently been fully open, but will now be changed to only let neutrons through with
a divergence of ±0.4◦and ±0.2◦. This was done for three different PSC opening times (0.1 ms, 0.5 ms
and 1 ms), for four different scattering angles (5◦, 45◦, 95◦, 135◦), and three different energy transfers
(0 meV, 1 meV, 10 meV). The full results can be found in Appendix C.2, but highlights of the results
are shown here. A figure showing a projection of the covariance matrix across different setting is shown
in Figure 5.19.
It is very clear that there is a much better Qx resolution as one closes the jaws, as one would expect,
and other resolution components do not significantly change, also as expected. From the figure, as well
as the DB values, the models agree with each other, with the earlier issue of slightly underestimating
the γi resolution still playing a factor for the open jaws. However, the divergence for the jaws allowing
±0.4◦divergence, seems to slightly overestimate the covariance matrix. Nonetheless, with these small
adjustments, and a lot more testing for edge cases, jaws will be as well-verified as the res of the resolution
model.

5.3
Discussion
The results presented in this work span a wide range of configurations and metrics, revealing general
agreement between theory and simulations , with subtle discrepancies. This section explores the key
factors influencing these outcomes, with emphasis on the statistical stability of covariance estimates, the
assumptions underlying the analytical model, and the physical correlations that shape the resolution
function. Furthermore, the inherent limitations to high-dimensional simulations, such as computational
cost, sampling density, and systematic effects, are addressed and their implications for model validation
and experimental design are discussed.
5.3.1
Assumptions and Correlations
Throughout this work, several simplifying assumptions were made to construct the resolution model.
The most fundamental assumption was that the resolution function is a multivariate Gaussian. However,
as shown in Figure 5.20, this assumption does not always hold. This figure shows the simulated reso-
lution distributions, and a Gaussian function which is centered at ( ⃗Q0, ∆E0) with covariance matching
the theoretical model
Examining individual components of the resolution function, the Qx resolution initially appears unaf-
fected by increasing PSC opening time, apart from a rise in intensity. At low energy transfer, Figure 5.20
a), the distributions look quite Gaussian, however, it is hard to be sure, since they have quite high statis-
tical noise. At high PSC opening times, a flat top appears between Qx = −1.56 and −1.54, resembling
the shape observed in the ∆E distribution under similar conditions. This might hint that even though
the PSC opening time does not included in the calculation of the Qx resolution, it may still have an
impact. Although PSC opening time is not explicitly included in the Qx resolution calculation, its im-
pact may still be present. Overall, the Gaussian approximation slightly underestimates the distribution
width.
The Qz resolution distribution for low energy transfer fits the Gaussian perfectly, with maybe a slight
overestimation of the amplitude. Nonetheless, in this regime for Qz the Gaussian approximation is valid.
However, when increasing the energy transfer, and for high PSC opening times, the approximation falls
apart again. For 0.1 ms the approximation still holds quite well, but at 0.5 ms a flat top starts to develop
on the distribution, which is increased even further for 1 ms. Here the Gaussian quite under estimates

the shape of the distribution.
A quite analogous thing can be said for the ∆E distributions.
The one caveat is that the 0.1 ms
low energy transfer curve also has quite a flat top, and the Gaussian distribution underestimates this
distribution as well. This might have to do with the energy distribution accepted at the sample position
not being exactly Gaussian. On the other hand, the high energy transfer energy resolution distributions
at high PCS opening times are very clearly square functions, and the Gaussian approximation falls short
of approximating the distribution correctly.
While the resolution of components in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space are defined by the standard deviations of the
distributions, which fit correctly, assuming that they are Gaussian when fitting or modeling will lead
to large discrepancies.
This issue is even more pronounced for the 2D projections as shown in Figure 5.21. While the low
energy transfer and low PSC opening time can be approximated as a Gaussian distributions, at higher
energy transfer and high PSC opening time, the resolution function cannot be approximated to this.
Here the distributions look much more like parallelograms or squares This is quite clearly seen in
the energy distribution, which like seen in Figure 5.20 is quite flat.
The 2σ projected covariance
ellipses do fit the majority of the distributions quite well, ever so slightly over estimating the ∆E
resolution. However, these 2σ contours do not say much about the actual shape of the distributions..
One thing that is interesting to compare is that for a square and a Gaussian distribution with the same

is much more complicated than a simple multivariate Gaussian. A possibility is that the cross-sectional

==PAGE:50==
5.3 Discussion
44
distribution for a single variable is Gaussian, but much more work needs to be done here to consider
all of this.
5.3.1.1
Beyond Gaussian Approximations
One possible correction to this Gaussian approximation is to incorporate higher moment into the res-
olution model to enable distribution reconstruction [26]. The second moment, or variance, is defined
as the expectation value of the squared deviation from the mean. Higher moments are defined as the
expectation value of the n’th power of the deviation from the mean. However, this approach faces two
major challenges: Many distributions can share similar moment sets; and higher-order moments derived
from Monte Carlo simulations are themselves noisy, limiting reliability.
Another possibility would be to combine parametric Gaussian modeling with corrections based on
simulated higher moments. However, calculating the higher moments from theoretical values and not
just fitting would require deriving analytic expressions for the instrument response, which is non-trivial
given the complexity of the system.
A more pragmatic strategy is to tabulate resolution shapes at a grid of points in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space, and
apply interpolation for intermediate points while retaining theoretical control over standard deviations.
This approach balances accuracy, computational cost and complexity.
5.3.1.2
Neglected Correlations
Another strong simplifying assumption was that neutron path parameters are independent. This is
demonstrably false. For example, correlations between Qx, and Qx and ∆E, as shown in Section 5.2.5.2,
depend strongly on incident energy which are not captured in the current model. These correlations
possibly arise from couplings to incident divergence, γi, with another parameter such as time-of-flight or
scattering angle. Resolving this requires a detailed multivariate analysis, as neglecting these correlations
can bias covariance predictions.
Additional correlations are expected between sample size and divergence. For instance, as shown in
Figure 5.3, sample width correlates with horizontal incident divergence. Along with this, from (5.2) it
is known that the accepted in-plane scattering angle is sample size dependent. A realistic resolution
model must therefore include sample geometry explicitly. Similarly, correlations between horizontal
and vertical divergence and minor couplings between instrument lengths, L1 and L2, and the angular
components, (γi,δi) and (θs,δf), should be looked into, even though some of them may be very small
and in the end negligible.
Finally, all primary simulations were performed for a single incident energy, with the choppers timed
such that the target energy corresponded to the center of the wavelength band. However, it remains to
be examined whether the edges of the wavelength band exhibit different resolution properties.
5.3.2
Variance and Simulation Limitations
While McStas provides realistic simulations, several limitations arise related to statistical accuracy and
computational cost.
5.3.2.1
Variance of Variances
As discussed in Section 5.2.7, the larger space a distribution contains, the more points are necessary to
sample the entire space properly. This naturally raises the question: How reliable are the covariance
estimates obtained from the sampled data?
This is an important statistical sampling problem and has to do with the central moments of distri-
butions. The variance has been used throughout this project, as the second moment of the resolution
function, which is being considering as a normalized probability function. To evaluate the reliability of
this variance estimate, the variance of the sample variance is introduced. This quantity describes the ex-
pected fluctuation in the estimated variance due to finite sampling. For a one-dimensional distribution,
==PAGE:51==
5.3 Discussion
45
the variance of the sample variance, V(σ2
N), can be expressed as [27]:
V(σ2
N) =

κ −N −3
N −1
 σ4
N

(5.8)
where σ2

N and κ
is the kurtosis of the distribution, which is given as:
E((Xi −µ)4) = κσ4
(5.9)
where σ2 is the variance of the true distribution, and the entire expectation value is the fourth moment
of the distribution.
Kurtosis is a measure of the “tailedness” of a distribution, or the tendency to
produce outliers. For a Gaussian distribution, this value is by definition 3. As such, the variance of the
resolution sample variance is:
V(σii,N) =

This adjustment highlights an important point: the effective samples size strongly influences the relia-
bility of the covariance estimates. Consequently, resolution functions that broaden significantly (e.g.,
at higher energy transfers or with tighter collimation settings) require many more effective samples to
achieve comparable statistical certainty.
5.3.2.2
Computational Constraints and Practical Challenges
Moving from 0 meV to 50 meV energy transfer increases the energy resolution, σ∆E, by roughly a factor
of 7. Because the variance of the sample variance scales as σ4, this implies that the effective number of
samples must increase by a factor of approximately 2,400 to maintain the same statistical certainty.
While this is theoretically possible, such an increase runs into the problem of necessary compute. For
reference, a simulation to measure the resolution function at a single point (not including run-time for
the primary spectrometer), was ∼1 CPU hours, for 109 neutrons simulated. This yields an effective
sample size of ∼2 · 103 for 0 meV and ∼1 · 103 for 25 meV on a single detector element for the lowest
PSC opening time and open jaws. This highlights a double challenge: high-energy neutrons not only
traverse the primary spectrometer with lower probability, but also require populating a much broader
distribution in ( ⃗Q, ∆E) space.
A similar trend occurs for PSC opening time. Increasing PSC time broadens resolution in Qz and ∆E,
but the extra neutron flux does not scale enough to offset the statistical loss. For example, doubling
PSC time from 0.1 ms to 0.2 ms increases resolution width by about 1.5× (requiring 5× more samples)
while flux only grows by ∼1.5×. This imbalance makes it increasingly ineﬀicient to obtain suﬀicient
statistics at higher PSC settings.
The opposite trend happens for the jaws.
Narrowing the jaws improves Qx resolution but reduces
flux, lowering effective sample size.
These changes affect all covariance components simultaneously,
complicating uniform sampling strategies further.
To illustrate the problem, one could consider two cases: Case 1: PSC opening time = 0.1 ms, ∆E =0 meV
for the 5 meV analyzer; Case 2: PSC opening time = 1.0 ms, ∆E =25 meV , for the 3.8 meV analyzer,
as seen in Figure 5.20. In case 2 the effective sample size is 3.6 times that of case 1, but the energy
resolution for case 2, 0.275 meV, is a factor 22 times than that of case 1, 0.0125 meV. The variance of
the energy resolution, using the Gaussian approximation, in case 1 is 2.5 · 10−11meV4, while in case 2
it is 1.5 · 10−6meV4.
==PAGE:52==
5.3 Discussion
46
While the variance on the energy resolution is 5 orders of magnitude larger, the question becomes:
When is variance “small enough” for validation purposes? The resolution function shown in Figure 5.21
appears visually to indicate the opposite of the above conclusion. However, visual completeness can be
misleading. Because the variance of covariance estimates scales as σ4, case 2 requires orders of magnitude
more effective samples to achieve comparable precision. In contrast, case 1, despite appearing sparse, is
concentrated in a small ellipsoid where each event carries far greater statistical weight. This underscores
that color plots should be interpreted with caution: Apparent density does not guarantee statistical
robustness. This example emphasizes the need to evaluate statistical precision quantitatively rather
than relying on visual impressions. Without accounting for how variance scales with resolution width,
misleading conclusions about convergence and reliability can easily arise.
Examining Figure 5.20, specifically the Qz resolution, reinforces the argument of distribution uncer-
tainty. For 25 meV the shape is slightly ambiguous, as it is unclear if the distributions is uniform, super
Gaussian or something in between. Resolving such ambiguities would require substantially higher statis-
tics, illustrating the impact of variance in the covariance matrix. Ultimately, acceptable variance of
sample variances should not be judged by absolute thresholds alone, but rather in the context of the dis-
tribution’s shape, and the level of precision required for meaningful validation. Statistical uncertainty
must therefore be assessed relative to the goals of the analysis, not merely the apparent completeness
of visualizations.
5.3.2.3
Memory and Runtime constraints
Detailed simulations with fine sampling over multi-dimensional parameter spaces (e.g., energy transfer,
scattering angle, PSC opening time) are constrained by memory, storage, and runtime, particularly on
shared HPC resources. Key limitations include:
• Storage requirements: Each simulation produces large output files, from ∼100 MB at 0.1 ms open-
ing time to over 1 GB at 1 ms. Consequently, the parameter space is sampled more densely at
shorter PSC times due to storage constraints. The resolution ellipse validation in total consumed
∼5 TB.
• Compute considerations: With a total of 240 cores available at a given time for one user and each
resolution ellipse costs ∼1 CPU-hour, there is a limit to how finely the parameter space can be
swept.
• Parameter space size: Comprehensive validation across energy transfer, scattering angle, and PSC
opening time is prioritized, while tube/channel and jaw variations receive sparser coverage.
From this several methodological question arises: Which areas of parameter space should a model
validation prioritize? Are the chosen parameter-space boundaries physically meaningful and useful for
scientific purposes? Clear criteria for these choices are essential to balance computational feasibility
with the need for robust model validation, and should be considered carefully for future validation.
While this validation focused on validating a wide range in parameter space, future validations should
focus on the edge cases for high scattering angles and high energy transfer for high and low PSC setting,
as these are the areas where more work needs to be done.
Ultimately, all of these challenges illustrate a fundamental trade-off in Monte Carlo methods: achieving
statistically reliable Monte Carlo resolution functions for all parameter sets is computationally expen-
sive. Practical modeling will therefore require a combination of variance-reduction techniques, adaptive
sampling strategies, and selective high-fidelity simulations rather than brute-force sampling.
==PAGE:53==
6 Resolution Effects on Phonon Dispersion
47
CHAPTER 6
Resolution Effects on
Phonon Dispersion
Accurate interpretation of inelastic neutron scattering data requires accounting for the instrument
resolution, which is convolved with the sample’s dynamic structure factor. Neglecting this effect can
lead to systematic errors in the position and width of features. This is particularly important when
the intrinsic features of the sample are comparable in width to the instrument resolution, making it
diﬀicult to distinguish between sample properties and instrumental broadening
In this chapter, we combine the theoretical phonon dispersion model with Monte Carlo convolution to
evaluate its impact on measurable spectra, and compare the convolution against full McStas simulations
to assess consistency and highlight the strengths and limitations of both approaches.
6.1
Theoretical Phonon Dispersion
The phonon model employed in this work follows the approach utilized in McStas’ Phonon_sample
component, based on G.L. Squires’ Introduction to the Theory of Thermal Neutron Scattering [29]. This
model describes a single 3D acoustic phonon branch for a face-centered cubic (fcc) lattice, accounting
for only first-nearest neighbor interactions.
While this representation captures essential features of acoustic phonons, it lacks the complexity of
real system models, and neglects higher order interactions. However, this model was chosen for this
simplicity and the direct comparison to McStas, factors that make it well-suited for validating the
convolution approach.
The material chosen for this study is lead (Pb), which provides a suitable test case due to its relatively
low maximum phonon energy of approximately 8 meV. This makes it simple to tailor a primary spec-
trometer simulations which includes the elastic line for the 5 meV analyzer and can capture the peak of
the dispersion. The acoustic phonon dispersion is modeled as:
ω( ⃗Q) = c
a
q
12 −J( ⃗Q) , where J( ⃗Q) =
X
⃗d
cos( ⃗Q · ⃗d)
(6.1)
where c is the speed of sound in the crystal, a is the lattice constant and ⃗Q is the momentum transfer
vector in the sample reference frame. ⃗d is the position of the 12 first-nearest-neighbors:
⃗d±i,±j = a
2(±ˆi ± ˆj) ,
ˆi, ˆj ∈{ˆx, ˆy, ˆz} ,ˆi ̸= ˆj
(6.2)
For lead, the constants are set to be c = 10meV · Å and a = 4.95 Å. Along with this, we will set the
sample temperature to T = 290 K and a cylindrical sample with height and diameter 2.5 mm.
6.1.1
Weighting Factors in the Phonon Cross Section
In the Phonon_sample implementation, the scattering weight is expressed as a product of several phys-
ically motivated factors:
• Absorption factor: Accounts for attenuation of the neutron beam inside the sample due to ab-
sorption and scattering along the incident and exit paths.
==PAGE:54==
6.2 Resolution Orientation and Sample Frame
48
• Geometric focusing factor: Reflects the probability of selecting a specific scattering channel among
nf possible final states and includes the illuminated sample volume and solid angle coverage.
• Kinematic cross-section factor: Incorporates the main dynamical dependencies, including the ratio
of final to initial neutron velocities (vf/vi), the Debye–Waller factor (DW), the 1/|ω| scaling from
the phonon density of states, and the Bose population factor.
• Jacobian factor: Arises from integrating over the delta function, correcting for the fact that energy
and momentum are not independent variables in inelastic scattering.
• Structural factor: Depends on the square of the scattering length b and the atomic mass M of
the target material.
In this study, only the dominant cross-section term, which will be called p3, is included, as it captures
the key kinematic dependencies of phonon scattering. Other factors, such as absorption and focusing,
were omitted for eﬀiciency, as the goal is to validate the convolution method rather than absolute
intensities. The Jacobian term would improve accuracy by correcting the intensity for steep dispersion
regions, but was excluded for simplicity.
6.2
Resolution Orientation and Sample Frame
Earlier discussions focused on projecting the resolution function into different planes, which is useful for
characterization as a normalized Gaussian. However, when assessing its effect on a dispersion, both the
effective amplitude (i.e., the weight during convolution) and the orientation of the resolution function
are critical. This section addresses these aspects and explains how the resolution function is rotated
from the laboratory to the sample reference frame. The notation is changed from ∆E to ω, as the focus
is now on a dispersion relation.
6.2.1
Amplitude
In general, the measured intensity I on a neutron spectrometer, represents the scattering function
S( ⃗Q, ω) convoluted with the resolution function R:
I( ⃗Q0, ω0) =

When approximating the resolution function as a multivariate gaussian distribution, the resolution
function can be written as:
R( ⃗Q −⃗Q0, ω −ω0) = R0( ⃗Q0, ω0) exp

6.2.1.1
Incident Intensity Spectrum
The monitors on BIFROST are normalized with respect to the start of each moderator pulse at a
repetition rate of 14 Hz. As seen in Figure 6.1 Left, slow neutrons from the previous pulse overlap
with fast neutrons from the current pulse within a given moderator frame at the sample position. It
is therefore crucial to re-normalize the bins to obtain the correct intensity spectrum, as shown in
Figure 6.1 Right. This spectrum can be expressed as p(ki) or, equivalently, as p(Ei) for convenience.
In the current analysis, the correction factor ∆Ωi∆ti/t4
i was not included in the calculation of Vi due
to time constraints in the implementation of the convolution framework.
6.2.1.2
Outgoing Intensity Spectrum
By replacing the sample with a simple flat source covering all analyzers uniformly and normalizing
the recorded neutron weight by the weight at the source, a comparative intensity distribution can be
obtained for the five analyzers, as well as the different channel types (index 1, 2 and 3). Table 6.1 shows
the relative increase in neutron ray weight from the sample position to the central detector element for
different analyzers and channels.
6.2.2
Orientation
The principal axes of the resolution ellipsoid are generally rotated relative to (Qx, Qz, ω) due to cor-
relations. This orientation determines whether the resolution broadens or sharpens a given feature
along the dispersion. Aligning ellipsoids with the dispersion minimizes effective broadening, whereas
misalignment leads to significant smearing. This loss of information can be critical when interpreting
resolution effects on dispersions. Thus, understanding ellipsoid orientation relative to the measured
feature is essential for accurate data interpretation.
To preserve some sense of this three-dimensional nature in 2D plots, we introduce a notation: a “+” or
“–” symbol is used to indicate whether the distribution is primarily above or below the mean value in
the projected-out axis on a given half of the projection. This is illustrated in Figure 6.2, which shows
the full 3D resolution ellipsoid, its 2D projections onto coordinate planes, and the use of these markers.
Small dashed lines are also included as orientation aids which splits the projection in two. This dashed
line also represents the line on which the distribution is exactly at the mean of the projected-out axis.
As an example of the “+”/“-” markers and dashed line, looking at the projection in Qz and Qx in
==PAGE:56==
6.3 Monte Carlo Convolution

planes are shown, with “+” and “–” markers indicating whether the ellipsoid extends
primarily above or below the mean of the projected-out variable. Dashed lines denote
equal extension of the projected-out variable above and below the mean.
Figure 6.2, the dashed line represents the line in the distribution where ω = 5 meV. The + side of the
distribution is on average above 5 meV and the minus side is below.
6.2.3
Sample Reference Frame
Converting the covariance matrix from the laboratory reference frame to the crystal reference frame is
as simple as doing a rotation, much like for ⃗Q Σsample = R · Σlab · RT . Here R is the same rotation
matrix from (3.14), with an extra dimension, for ω, that is 1 in the diagonal, similar to Qy.
6.3
Monte Carlo Convolution
With the instrument resolution fully defined in terms of its covariance matrix Σ and normalization
amplitude R0, the next step is to perform the convolution of the phonon dispersion with this resolution
function. Direct analytical convolution is generally intractable for realistic dispersions due to the high
dimensionality of the problem. Therefore, a Monte Carlo (MC) integration approach is employed.
As discussed in Section 4.1.1, MC integration is well suited for high-dimensional integrals such as the
convolution of a phonon dispersion with a 4D resolution function. The convolution integral at a point
( ⃗Q0, ω0) can be approximated as:
I( ⃗Q0, ω0) =
ZZ
S( ⃗Q, ω) R( ⃗Q −⃗Q0, ω −ω0) d ⃗Q dω ≈1

6.3.2
Single Mode Approximation
A more eﬀicient approach exploits the delta distribution in S( ⃗Q, ω) by integrating over ω analytically.
This reduces the integral to a three-dimensional form:
I( ⃗Q0, ω0) =

Then in practice, the integration utilizes importance sampling again, however, since we are now unable

I( ⃗Q0, ω0) ≈1

(6.13)
Where D is our proposed gaussian, which uses the same covariance matrix as the resolution function,
but with the ω axis projected out. This reduces the dimensionality by one and concentrates sampling
to be on the distribution. This methodology is called the single mode approximation (SMA), as it only
approximates a single mode [32].
As seen in Figure 6.3 Bottom, the SMA method converges significantly faster and with much smaller
variance than the naive 4D approach. In addition, the computational time per convolution is reduced
by roughly 2.5 orders of magnitude for the same accuracy, as shown in Figure 6.4. For the phonon
convolution we will utilize this method with a sample count of N = 104, such each of our convolutions
has an statistical uncertainty of ∼1%.
==PAGE:59==
6.4 Methodology
53
6.4
Methodology
The methodology consists of two parts: 1) McStas Simulations and data reduction using the BIFROST_nD
class and (2) Resolution convolution using SMA integration. Both of the approaches were implemented
on DTU’s HPC, utilizing the administrator-worker approach discussed in Section 4.1.5.
6.4.1
Simulations
The methodology for the McStas simulations is quite simple. Firstly, a single primary spectrometer
simulation was run, choosing the chopper delays such that the energy range of Ei ∈[4.95, 14.5] meV
would arrive at the sample position. The PSC opening time and jaw settings were varied to give a good
taste for how different settings impact measured dispersion. For the secondary spectrometer, to ensure
continuous angular coverage, a combination of two fixed A4 angles and a continuous scan of the sample
rotation angle, A4. Due to the fourfold symmetry of the face-centered cubic (fcc) lattice around the
(001) axis, a 90◦rotation in A3 provides complete sampling of the phonon structure in reciprocal space.
Choosing the correct A4 and A3 values is determined by the sample structure.
Since the phonon
dispersion repeats every 2 brillouin zones in (HKL), an ideal place to look for the dispersion would be
at the (020) reflection. For the 5 meV analyzer, this point is at 2θ ≈110◦. As such setting two A4
angles of 110◦and 115◦will be used. This is not exactly realistic, as the maximum scattering angle for
BIFROST is 135◦, and the maximum scattering angle of these simulations is 160◦, but for the sake of
this project, this will be ignored. The A3 angles set will be centered around A3m = 2θ
2 = 55, and with
the four fold symmetry, the A3 range will be 10◦to 100◦degrees. To make the simulations as fine as
possible, an A3 step of 0.5◦was used, so there are 181 simulations per A4 angle.
All simulations were performed for the complete set of analyzers in the instrument, corresponding to
five distinct final energies.
Each analyzer dataset spans the full energy range and is treated as an
independent measurement. For the analysis presented in this work, the 5 meV analyzer was selected as
the reference case, but it is important to remember that five data sets are available.
6.4.2
Convolution
The convolution was carried out for every detector element, tube and channel combination for the 5 meV
analyzer, which totals 2,700 detector elements per analyzer. The integration is done for each detector
element for each A3 and A4 angle, which further increases the convolution calculation count to ∼106.
When incorporating energy transfer into the calculation, the situation becomes significantly more com-
plex. Simulated data are converted into visualizations by binning events into voxels (3D pixels) in
(H, K, ω) space. As a result, each pixel in a 2D histogram inherently includes integration effects from
the dimension that has been projected out, meaning the displayed intensity represents an average over
a finite range in the third dimension. While the angular contributions cannot increase further than the
natural binning of the detector elements, when specifying the energy transfers to convolve the model
with, this has to be considered. If one simply chose the mid points of each voxel, these integration
effects would not play a part. Taking this into consideration, five convolution calculations per detector
element per width of the (H, K, ω) voxel in energy will be done to ensure several energy contributions
per voxel. To simplify it, if we are using 0.1 meV energy bin width with midpoint on 0 meV, our con-
volution will calculate convolutions for all detector elements with energy transfers, -0.05, -0.25, 0, 0.25
and 0.5 meV. For the incident energy range discussed in the McStas methodology, and an energy bin
width of 0.05 meV, the number of energy transfer points that need to be considered is ∼1,000.
As such in total, ∼109 convolution calculations have to be done for a fulfilling model. This is a massive
computational burden, and even for convolutions that take <1 ms, this would take over 11 days for
a single CPU core to calculate, or 11 CPU days. To avoid this, a stochastic sampling approach was
adopted. For each point, a random number p was drawn from a uniform distribution from 0 to 1, and the
convolution was computed only if p exceeded a predefined threshold. This probabilistic down-sampling
reduces the number of computed points while preserving statistical representativeness of the integrated
signal. We chose the threshold to be 0.9, thus only computing 10% of all convolutions.
==PAGE:60==
6.4 Methodology
54
Figure 6.5: Slices of phonon dispersion comparing convolution, first and third columns, and McStas simulations, second and fourth columns. Top) ω slices at 2 meV
and 5 meV (integration widths 0.10 meV and 0.16 meV). Middle and Bottom) H and K slices at selected values (integration widths 0.05 meV in ω). All
slices are for PSC opening time of 1 ms and open jaws to ensure enough McStas statistics and integration width of 0.016 r.l.u. in H, K.

6.5 Results and Visualization
55
6.5
Results and Visualization
This section compares the convolution method with McStas simulations in several ways. Firstly, 2D
slices of the phonon dispersion are shown in Figure 6.5; secondly, 1D line cuts are presented in Figure 6.6.
Overall, as shown in Figure 6.5, the two methods demonstrate excellent agreement. The widths of
the various features in each curve match remarkably well, and the influence of the resolution on the
intensity at different points along the dispersion is also well captured. This is illustrated best by the
slice at ω =2 meV, where the top right corner is more intense than the bottom right. The McStas data
is comparatively sparse, which makes it more challenging to compare slices at high energy transfers.
This is illustrated in the top-right panel of Figure 6.5. There the number of events in the dispersion
centered at (020) is very small. A potential solution would be to integrate over larger bins, although
this might also smear out some of the resolution effects.
The phonon dispersion was also simulated for PSC opening times of 0.1 ms and 0.5 ms, as well as with
open jaws and jaws set to ±4◦and ±2◦. However, the number of simulated neutrons for these settings
was significantly lower than in the data presented here. Results for these settings and the convolutions
are shown in Appendix B, with the same integration slices.
Considering the individual line cuts in Figure 6.6 and the corresponding FWHM values in Table 6.2, the
model performs well very well, though some refinements are still needed. Most peaks differ by less than
10%, indicating very good agreement overall. There are, however, a few outliers—most notably the
high-H peak for ω = 5, K= −2.2, which deviates significantly. As seen in Figure 6.6, this discrepancy
may stem from low sampling in that region, as evidenced by the large McStas error bars.

for each panel. All intensities are scaled to unity for comparison.

Table 6.2: Comparison of peak positions (µx) and widths (FWHM) for convolution (first data line in
each block) and McStas simulations (second data line) across different line cuts. For line
cuts with two peaks, low and high indicate the smaller and larger values of the variable not
fixed in the cut. The line cuts correspond to the cuts in Figure 6.6.
.
The convolution data also exhibits large error bars. These represent the standard deviation of points
within each bin, meaning that larger bin sizes naturally lead to larger error bars, clearly seen in the
rightmost column. This might hint that 5 energy calculations per energy bin was too many, or that
even less than 10% of the convolutions are necessary for a good convolution result. Furthermore, the
intensities for each cut were scaled to unity. This normalization is necessary because additional weighting
factors from the phonon cross-section calculation were not included in the convolution. These missing
weighting factors appear to affect certain cases, particularly the cut at (ω = 5meV, K= −2.2 r.l.u),
where the low-H peak is convolved to a much lower intensity than shown in the simulations. However,
this discrepancy could also be influenced by integration effects, as the McStas error bars are notably
large for this peak.
The main deviation is seen in the peak centers, µ, which shift toward more positive values, particularly
at higher energy transfers.
This arises from an overestimation of Ei in the BIFROST_nD code, as
illustrated in Figure 6.7. Although the simulated primary spectrometer flight path is slightly shorter
than the nominal value, the measured difference in average path length between 2.5 meV and 50 meV
is only 5 mm, far too small to account for the observed error. Since the final energy and t2 show no
discrepancies, and identical conversion constants are used in both BIFROST_nD and McStas, the issue
likely stems from a small offset in the McStas time-of-flight calculation relative to the PSC opening
midpoint.
This is puzzling, as t1 is defined as ToF −t2−tP SC, where tP SC is determined directly from the chopper
settings and t2 matches the expected nominal value. This suggests that the discrepancy originates from
how McStas records ToF. Further investigation is required, as this error is important for accurate data
Figure 6.7: Relative error of Ei depending on incident energy. The relative error is the calculated
value minus the actual value.
==PAGE:63==
6.6 Discussion
57
analysis. However, it should not affect the resolution calculation beyond introducing a slight shift in
the peak positions.
Another observation is the presence of gaps in the dispersion at locations where measurements were
taken. This is most evident in the bottom-right panel of Figure 6.8, which shows the projection of
all data points with H∈[0, 1] onto a single plane.
A distinct arc of missing data originates from
the dispersion point at K= −2.05 and ω = 3.5meV. This appears to be a numerical artifact of the
Phonon_simple model and is treated as such.
6.6
Discussion
Returning to the main focus of this project, the projected resolution function is overlaid on three slices
of the dispersion, as shown in Figure 6.8.
These resolution functions were calculated by selecting
all neutrons contributing to a small volume around a point and averaging their resolution functions.
This step is necessary because multiple points, with different A3 angles, scattering angles, and energy
transfers, can contribute to a single pixel bin. Each of these variables affects the shape and orientation
of the resolution function, and this variation must be accounted for.
For the ω = 2 meV slice, the dispersion mostly lies within the 2σ contour and is noticeably broader in
regions where the dispersion is perpendicular to the resolution ellipsoid. Due to the tilt of the resolution
in and out of the ω plane (as indicated by the “+/-” markers), the top-right corner is expected to be
better resolved than the bottom-left corner, which agrees with the simulations.
Shifting focus onto the H= −0.15 r.l.u slice, the resolution functions again fit very well with the
dispersion widths. The one place it does not is at the bottom of the dispersion, which is due to the
H integration impacting this area the most, as that is where the gradient of the dispersion out of
the plane is greatest. The orientation of the resolution ellipsoids is consistent with previous results
Figure 6.8: Top row and Bottom Left) Three slices of the phonon dispersion with overlaid resolution
functions. The ellipses align with the local curvature of the dispersion, broadening mainly
perpendicular to it. Bottom Right) Projection of all neutron events with H∈[0, 1] to show
the Phonon_simple artifact.
==PAGE:64==
6.6 Discussion
58
(positive towards negative K and positive H), except near the bottom of the dispersion, where the
orientation switches abruptly.
This abrupt switch is quite interesting, and shows how the effect of
different scattering angles and sample rotations causes the resolution function to vary quite a bit in
HKL space quickly. These two points are not significantly different in scattering angle from neighboring
points, 2◦from K= −2.2 and 9◦from K= 1.9, but because of the rotation of the covariance matrix by
A3, this switch occurs here.
Lastly, looking at the constant K= −2.2 r.l.u slice, the resolution functions appear to slightly overesti-
mate the dispersion width. This is likely due to the low neutron statistics, particularly for positive H
values as the 2σ contours should contain 95% of the data points. Nevertheless, the observed tilt of the
dispersion is physically reasonable: the resolution function is aligned with the dispersion for positive H
values and perpendicular for negative H values. Overall, the resolution model shows good agreement
with the data but requires minor refinements to fully capture the observed features.
6.6.1
Systematic Correlations and Data Combination
All secondary spectrometer simulations at a given A3/A4 angle combination were generated from the
same initial primary spectrometer simulation MCPL file containing 109 particles.
As a result, the
individual datasets are not entirely independent, and small correlations between the resulting spectra
may arise. While this effect is expected to be minor compared to statistical uncertainties, it could lead
to subtle biases when combining the data. A straightforward way to mitigate this issue would be to
run larger, independent primary spectrometer simulations for each analyzer setting, thereby reducing
potential correlations.
Furthermore, the data analysis in this work relied exclusively on the 5 meV analyzer dataset, with
the other analyzer datasets being ignored.
While this was acceptable for the present simulations,
future studies would benefit from combining all analyzer datasets in a consistent and meaningful way.
One possible approach would be to weight each data point by the inverse of the determinant of the
local covariance matrix in ( ⃗Q, ω) space. This is crucial because the different analyzers have distinct
resolution functions, and a simple unweighted average could cause the broader 5 meV analyzer resolution
to dominate, or even produce non-Gaussian artifacts in line cuts. Addressing these aspects will be
important for ensuring that future data is utilized as effectively and accurately as possible.
6.6.2
Computational Eﬀiciency
One of the uses of a resolution convoluting calculations is as a fast and simple way to: A) Visualize
what the data should look like for a sample before the beam time, or B) To fit model parameters by
comparing resolution-convolved predictions to experimental line cuts or slices. For this to be useful, the
convolution method has to be more eﬀicient than McStas simulations, fast enough to enable iterative
fitting or rapid exploration of model parameters during analysis or even in preparation for beam time.
This section will talk about the runtime, memory usage (RAM usage) and storage usage (disk usage)
of the two different methods: Convolution and McStas simulations
6.6.2.1
Convolution
As described in the methodology, a total of 108 points, representing 10% of the 109 simulated events,
needed to be convolved for the resolution-convolved phonon data presented in this work.
The cur-
rent implementation of the convolution procedure is highly brute-force, relying on nested for-loops
and non-vectorized matrix operations. Despite this ineﬀiciency, a single energy-transfer slice required
approximately 250 CPU s to compute, with each individual data point taking roughly 7.5 CPU ms. Con-
sequently, the entire 108-point volume was processed in about 70 CPU hours. Memory consumption per
slice peaked at 120 MB, resulting in a total of 120 GB of RAM used. Each slice is saved as a 3.8 MB
file, and the total memory usage for the entire (H, K, ω) space is 3.8 GB.
The present implementation is single-threaded, which limits scalability for more complex dispersions or
larger sampling amounts. Future improvements will focus on parallelization and, critically, vectorization
of integration routine for different points, which is expected to reduce runtime by at least one order of
magnitude. Along with this, for parameter fitting, the full (H, K, ω) space is not necessary. As such, it
is often more fitting to convolve individual slices of (H, K, ω) space instead. The current implementation
only allows for ω slices, but a next step would be to be able to select H or K slices instead to convolve.
==PAGE:65==
6.6 Discussion
59
6.6.2.2
McStas Simulations
For one of the 362 simulations for a full phonon dispersion, the average compute time was 7000 CPU s,
which means that for all 362 combinations of A3 and A4, the total simulation time is 703 CPU hours.
This is about a factor 10 more than the convolution. However, McStas benefits from built-in MPI
support; by distributing each simulation across 10 CPU cores, the effective wall-clock time was reduced
to a similar range as the convolution calculations.
It is worth noting that a significant fraction of the McStas runtime is spent writing Monitor_nD files,
a process that can occasionally cause simulations to crash due to a known error where the master MPI
rank fails to open a file before the worker ranks attempt to write. These crashes can increase the overall
runtime and reduce reliability. The memory usage per A3/A4 simulation was approximately 600 MB,
which totals 271 GB of RAM used. The total storage space used for the raw data for the entire secondary
spectrometer simulation is 600 MB, which when analyzed turns into 2 GB. The storage usage of the
individual A3/A4 paired simulations depends on the values of those two angles, as some simulations
have only a couple points, while others have thousands. However, the real storage usage for the McStas
simulations comes from the primary spectrometer, where the MCPL file is a total of 40 GB large.
6.6.2.3
Comparison
Even with its unoptimized implementation, the convolution approach achieves comparable performance
times to McStas, while avoiding some of the reliability issues associated with Monitor_nD output.
McStas remains highly robust for complex simulations, but the convolution method offers a simpler,
more direct path for testing models and fitting data. However, the McStas simulations also produces
data for all 5 analyzer energies, and as such, effectively, the compute time is cut in 5. To directly
compare the two simulation methods, both were set to queue on the HPC simultaneously. With 240
available cores per user, the 1000 convolutions, each using a single core, completed in slightly over 16
hours, whereas the McStas simulations, each utilizing 8 cores, took slightly under 24 hours to finish.
While this comparison is useful, it is also only relevant for people with HPC access, which not all users
may have.
Comparing memory usage is somewhat more nuanced, as the reported RAM reflects only the peak
usage on the HPC system and not the full memory footprint over time. Ideally, a comparative study of
the memory usage profile throughout the simulation would provide a more accurate picture. However,
given that the maximum RAM usage for the McStas simulations is approximately twice as large, it still
indicates a significantly higher memory demand and highlights the relative eﬀiciency of the convolution-
based approach.
The storage use for the two methods is quite interesting to compare. While the McStas secondary
spectrometer simulations utilizes significantly less memory than the convolutions, it is because the
data is in event format. The McStas monitors will not record the intensity at the points which were
measured but no intensity was recorded. Furthermore, the 2 GB of data is for all 5 analyzer energies,
which means it is 400 MB per analyzer. Meanwhile, the convolutions record a total intensity on 108
points using 64-bit floats. This means that even for points where there is no or negligible intensity, a
64-bit float is still allocated for it, which utilized quite a bit of space, totaling 3.8 GB for one analyzer
energy.
Despite this, the total storage still remains well below the 40 GB required for the McStas
primary spectrometer simulations. Although this large dataset can be reused for different secondary
spectrometer configurations, it still represents a substantial storage burden, particularly when the goal
is to rapidly fit models or explore experimental scenarios in advance.
Lastly, as mentioned, the same phonon dispersion was also measured for lower PSC opening setting
and jaws, however the number of events for these was much lower than the simulation showed. Here
a true positive of the convolution model appears, as it is not limited by the number of neutrons, and
thus produces just as clear slices as in Figure 6.5 for low PSC opening times and smaller jaw settings.
Future developments, such as implementing vectorized and parallelized routines and exploring multi-
mode approximations, could make the convolution method significantly faster than McStas. While a
multi-mode approximation would require denser Monte Carlo sampling (increasing computational cost),
it would eliminate the need for dedicated McStas components and instead rely on a lightweight sampling
function. With these optimizations, the convolution approach could reach run-times that are usable on
a laptop, similar to other established resolution-convolution frameworks in neutron scattering [32], [33].
==PAGE:66==
7 Conclusion
60
CHAPTER 7
Conclusion
This thesis has developed and validated a comprehensive resolution model for the BIFROST neutron
spectrometer, providing a framework capable of accurately characterizing the energy and momentum
resolution in ( ⃗Q, ℏω) space. By utilizing linear error propagation and constructing a full covariance ma-
trix, the resolution was represented as a multivariate Gaussian, enabling both a compact mathematical
description and straightforward visualization. The approach was systematically benchmarked against
Monte Carlo ray-tracing simulations with McStas, demonstrating that the analytical model captures
the essential features of the instrument’s resolution while offering computational eﬀiciency.
The resolution model agreed with simulations for a large range of different instrumental parameters,
scattering angles and energy transfers. The largest discrepancy was the correlations between Qx and
the other parameters, which is likely due to missing correlations, and the general Qx resolution, where
the resolution for small energy transfers was slightly underestimated (+5% residuals). Along with this,
it was shown that the gaussian covariance approximation works well for low energy transfers, but falls
short for high energy transfers and large PSC opening times.
The work also introduced a convolution-based method for single-mode scattering cross sections, using
a SMA convolution approach, which provides a fast and flexible alternative to McStas for resolution-
corrected data analysis. Although this method is not as comprehensive as McStas for modeling full
instrument behavior, it proved capable of delivering comparable results for well-defined test cases.
Notably, the analytical and convolution methods can be applied to large parameter scans or fitting
procedures, where the cost of full Monte Carlo simulations would be prohibitive, as well as calculate
dispersions for low neutron count settings much faster than McStas could for the same statistical cer-
tainty.
This thesis demonstrates that an analytical Gaussian resolution model, when paired with a carefully
parameterized covariance matrix, can serve as a reliable and computationally eﬀicient alternative to
full-scale Monte Carlo simulations for BIFROST. By quantifying the contributions of key instrument
parameters and validating them against McStas, the model provides a deeper understanding of how
different components shape the overall resolution.
Future Work
While significant progress has been made in this project, as well as in previous studies, there remain
many avenues for further development.
One important aspect concerns the resolution model itself.
The Qx resolution and its correlations with other parameters require a more detailed investigation,
particularly as current assumptions of independence between neutron path parameters may not hold
under all conditions. Sample size effects and their induced correlations are another area where more
work needs to be done to produce a more accurate modeling. Furthermore, looking into non-Gaussian
functions for the resolution could provide a more accurate description, especially in regimes where the
Gaussian assumption breaks down.
Looking at the data analysis, the most vital thing is that the incident energy Ei is correctly calculated,
as systematic errors in Ei could severely impact the analysis. This is largely the biggest error in this
project and something that should have had more time invested into it. Further work vectorizing and
parallelizing the convolution method would have made the comparison between McStas simulations and
the convolution much more stark.
Looking even further forwards with the convolution method, extending the current single-mode approach
to a multi-mode approximation would make the convolution framework applicable to more complex
scattering systems. Along with this, decreasing the storage size of the dataset, by using a different data
==PAGE:67==
7 Conclusion
61
structure, as well as allowing for H and K slices to be done would be a large step forward. Furthermore,
using more complex dispersions would show the complexities of the resolution function, and would be
something that could be used for the first science cases on BIFROST. A moon-shot idea to make the
convolution more eﬀicient, would be to implement non-linear convolution schemes, where important
regions of parameter space are sampled more densely, or where different detector element - energy
transfer combinations are sampled for importance sampling. This would speed up the convolutions and
use less memory.
Beyond these improvements, if the BIFROST model in McStas is to be used as a full digital twin
of the instrument, incorporating counting time analysis, as outlined in [5], is a must for comparing
experimental and simulated data sets. Along with this, combining data from the different analyzer
energies and performing detailed comparisons between the convolution method and McStas for various
dispersions would be a much needed step forward for this project, as treating each data set individually
is ineffective.
In the longer term, a robust fitting algorithm, like the ones used in Tobyfit [34], for dispersion param-
eters is also important to include. This project has only utilized the convolution method for a known
dispersion, but for unknown dispersions, fitting a model to the neutron data is important. One step
further in this direction would be to be able to de-convolve resolution effects from neutron data, either
using large neural networks, or utilizing methods from image analysis. This is a long and complex
idea, with no certainty of a useful result, but it could end up being incredibly rewarding for neutron
data-analysis methods in general.

sin(θ′) −sin(θ) cos(ϕ) = 0 →λ′

2d cos(ϕ) = 0
(A.14)
Which means that new wavelength will be λ′ = λ cos(ϕ), and for energy this equates to E′
f = Ef/ cos(ϕ).
==PAGE:70==
B Phonon Slices
64
APPENDIX B
Phonon Slices
This appendix shows the phonon slice comparison between convolution and simulation. All figures have
an additional text of ”Top) ω slices at 2 meV and 5 meV (integration widths 0.10 meV and 0.16 meV).
Middle and Bottom) H and K slices at selected values (integration widths 0.05 meV in ω). All slices
are for PSC opening time of 1 ms and open jaws to ensure enough McStas statistics and integration
width of 0.016 r.l.u. in H, K”, exactly as Figure 6.5 has.
As expected, the smaller the jaws opening and the smaller the PSC opening time, the fewer neutrons
come through. With the smallest jaw opening and smallest PSC opening time, see Figure B.3, there
are nearly no neutrons which arrive at the sample position. There is a clear improvement in the energy
resolution at higher energy transfers for lower PSC opening times as expected. The effect of smaller
jaws may not be seen due to large integration widths in K and H. This effect should be looked further
into.
